This report is based upon the findings of an apprisal mission which visited Guinea-Biuau in May 1992. The mission members consisted
of Mmes/Messrs. Carol Hoppy (Mission Leader/Task Manager), J. Angers (Research Analyst, AF4PH), L. Secco (Architect/Consultant),
V. Reis (Health Specialist/Consultant), and 1. Chabot (Public Health Specialist/Consultant). Mr. Yves Tencalla(Resident Representative
in Bisau) also participated in the mission and project development. Ms. Hoppy conducted a post-apprisl mission in Guinea-Bissau in
August/September 1992 to follow-up on conditions precedent to negotiations. Mr. E. Elmendorf, AFTPN, was the Lead Advisor for the
project. Peer reviewers included: Mr. G. Gauthier and Mr. S. Berkman, AFTED, for the health trmining component, and Mrs. L.
Domingo, AF4PH, for the social action fund and project management. The report was written by Ms. Hoppy, with contributions
submitted by the mission members. Mrs. G. Santos assisted in processing the report. Messrs. Ian Porter and Edwin R. Lim are the
managing Division Chief and Department Director, respectively, for the operation.

REPUBLIC OF GUINEA-BISSAU
SOCIAL SECTOR PROJECT
CREDIT AND PROJECT SUMMARY
Borrower:           Republic of Guinea-Bissau
Beneficiaries:      Ministries of Foreign Affairs and Cooperation, and Public Health
Credit Amoun:       SDR 6.4 Million (JS$8.8 Million)
Terms:              Standard, with 40 years maturity
Project Description:   The project would assist the Government in improving the delivery and
quality of primary health care for a wider base of the population, with a
special focus on the most vulnerable groups, particularly those at the
periphery in rural areas of the country. In order to achieve this objective the
project would finance high priority investment, through coordinating efforts
with other donor agencies, strengthening institutions and developing human
capacity building measures. The project's design should enhance dialogue and
create synergy among various social sector institutions and NGOs. In health,
the project would finance: (a) rehabilitation and equipping of health centers in
three regions, and health posts, through community participation, throughout
the country; (b) comprehensive support for in-service, local and short-term
training programs for all levels of health workers and professionals; and (c)
technical assistance and institutional strengthening inputs to coordinate and
strengthen the delivery of information, education and communications (IEC)
activities, develop an IEC policy framework, and suggest targeted programs.
The project would be cross-sectoral, and support international and national
NGOs through the establishment of a Social Action Fund (SAF) to help
expand and/or develop social sector micro-projects throughout the country.
and Risks:        The main benefit of the project would be to improve the delivery and quality
of primary health care for a greater proportion of the population, particularly
the most vulnerable groups at the periphery, and, thereby, have a positive
impact on the social indicators. Direct benefits of the proposed project will be
the physical and human capital improvements resulting from the subprojects,
and strengthening the capacity of several institutions charged with delivering
social services. Through the creation of a Social Action Fund, the project
would improve coordination among various entities including social sector
ministries, community groups, donors and NGOs, while promoting greater
regional and local participation. The SAF would reach those at the grassroots
levels and the poor through micro social sector projects, i.e., women in
development, nutrition, maternal and child health, family planning and skills
development.



- ii -
The proposed operation has two significant risks. The first risk involves the
limited managerial and implementation capabilities of the Government and the
primary institutions involved. This risk is being minimized by keeping the
subprojects simple in design. It is also being minimized by establishing a
Project Implementation Committee with technical representatives from entities
involved in the project, who will be specifically responsible for the
implementation of subprojects in the respective ministries. Overall project
management will be situated within a strong Project Coordination Unit located
in the Ministry of Foreign Affairs and Cooperation. The second risk is the
lack of sufficient counterpart funds and the overall financial sustainability of
the project. These risks will be addressed by setting targets for increases in
central budget allocations to the social sectors and monitoring social spending
as part of the project conditionality; in addition, to ensure sufficient
counterpart funds, local labor and materials will be provided through the
community participation element built into the project.

REPUBLIC OF GUINEA-BISSAU
SOCIAL SECTOR PROJECT
I. INTRODUCTION
1.1          Guinea-Bissau is a small country in West Africa with about one million inhabitants.
With an estimated 1991 per capita income of about US$190, the country is one of the poorest in the
world. The economy is characterized by a large traditional rural sector, producing primarily for
subsistence. Agriculture, fisheries and forestry account for about 90% of employment and an
estimated 50% of GDP. Most Guineans live in conditions of extreme poverty and social deprivation.
The social indicators for health, education and the status of women are among the world's worst.
1.2          The country gained its independence from Portugal in 1974, following a protracted
and economically devastating war which dislocated one-fifth of the population, destroyed an important
part of the economic infrastructure, and reduced output of the principal crops by more than one-third.
The liberation war also resulted in a breakdown of public administration and services as the Guineans
lacked the necessary technical and managerial skills to run the state institutions. In 1987, against a
background of a deteriorating economic situation, the Government prepared a comprehensive medium-
term economic adjustment program in close collaboration with the International Monetary Fund (IMF)
and the International Development Association (IDA). The program's aims in the medium-term were
to re-establish internal and external equilibria, stimulate growth, improve resource allocation, and
normalize relations with foreign creditors. Notable progress was made in the early stages of the
program. In 1988 and 1989, however, there was slippage in meeting fiscal and monetary targets.
While the Government continues to be committed to a program of structural adjustment to bring the
country back on a sustainable growth path, and is engaged in the process of promoting privatization in
the economy, there have been further slippage in program implementation since 1989 which has
delayed the release of the third tranche of the Second Structural Adjustment Credit. The next phase
in the Government's adjustment program (1992-94) is expected to focus on overcoming recent
slippage on the stabilization front; deepening the reform process; and further strengthening the public
and private sectors. There will also be increased focus on poverty alleviation, the provision of social
services, particularly for the population's most vulnerable groups, and the development of human
resources.
1.3           It is recognized that real progress in the productive sectors of the economy is tied to
progress in the social sectors and expansion of the human resource base. Accordingly, the
Government of Guinea-Bissau is attempting to improve the social sectors by developing the country's
human resources and improving the quality and access of health and education in the country. To
achieve these goals, the Government has requested IDA's assistance in financing a social sectors
project for the years 1993-97.



- 2 -
II. THE SOCIAL SECTORS IN GUINEA-BISSAU
A.    Overview
2.1           Guinea-Bissau has an array of complex social problems. The country, which has a
young population with about 45% under 15 years of age, has an illiteracy rate of about 70% and a
society comprising more than 30 ethnolinguistic groups. Life expectancy is less than 47 years. A
Guinean child born today has one of the lowest life expectancies in the world: 140 children in every
1,000 do not survive the first year of life. The population growth rate is estimated at about 2.5%
annually. (Basic data sheet and comparative indicators are found in Annex 2-1). Endemic
diseases are widespread. Approximately 90% of the demand on the health system is currently due to
a small number of high prevalence diseases such as malaria, respiratory infections, diarrhea and other
infections. Sexually transmitted diseases (STDs), especially gonorrhea, have a high prevalence. It is
estimated that about 10% of the country's adult population is HIV-positive for AIDS.
2.2           While Portuguese is the country's official language, it is estimated that fewer than
10% of the population understand or speak it. The primary school enrollment ratio is about 40%,
and is on a downward trend. Guinea-Bissau is among the 10 countries in the region with the lowest
primary enrollment ratios, and females represent only about one-third of the total enrolled. The
secondary school enrollment ratio is only 4.2% of the relevant age group, and only about 3% of girls
of secondary school age attend school. Less than 2% of all students entering the secondary system
actually complete it.
2.3         Girls tend to be married young, and begin having children typically while they are still
in their teens. Family planning awareness is low. Guinean women, because of their lack of education
and training and limited access to information and health services, are particularly disadvantaged.
They have limited opportunities for participating in the formal sector of the economy, and their wage
earning opportunities are severely restricted. While the women are responsible for much of the
agricultural work, they have less access to credit, markets, technology and information than do men.
Because of their lack of education, and their lack of access to information and health services, women
are frequently unaware of how to take more control over their lives and adopt basic measures and
practices in nutrition, hygiene, sanitation and family planning to improve their own welfare and that
of their children.
B.    Sectoral Issues
2.4           There are a number of common issues and constraints which affect the social sectors
including: effectiveness of the services, delivery of health and education, quality of services, internal
efficiency, equity and access, and sectoral financing. An analysis of these issues, highlighted below,
leads to the conclusion that a social sectors strategy should be built around priority elements which
include the development of human resources; the rehabilitation of existing facilities; provision of
essential inputs; and, development of a consolidated information, education and communications
campaign to increase public awareness about basic social sector issues and programs.
1.    Effectiveness
2.5           Guinea-Bissau's health and education systems have been unable to respond effectively
to the demands of the post-colonial period. The health care system has not been able to improve such



-3 -
health indicators as infant and maternal mortality rates. Similarly, the education system has been
unable to supply the trainable manpower needed to direct the economy towards sustainable growth.
2.     Efficiency
2.6           Delivery of services. Delivery of health services and basic education are adversely
affected by a scarcity of adequately trained health care personnel and teachers. Low salaries and an
inadequate career structure diminish motivation and force teachers and health care workers into taking
on other jobs simultaneously in order to make a living. Insufficient supervision and monitoring,
hindered by a lack of transportation and insufficient numbers of trained inspectors to visit schools and
medical personnel to visit remote rural areas, compound service delivery problems. Primary health
care (PHC), the centerpiece of the Government's health policy, is not working effectively because of
weak links within the system, particularly at the health center level which is responsible for training
and supervising workers at village level basic health posts.
2.7           In addition to staffing and supervision constraints, delivery of services is also affected
by the generally poor physical state of health and education facilities. There is minimum maintenance
of facilities and equipment in both sectors due to a lack of funds and skilled personnel. Many schools
and health facilities are in a state of dilapidation, without water and sanitation, and most are poorly
equipped lacking even basic supplies and materials. Many health facilities, especially those at the
periphery and charged with delivery of PHC, are handicapped by a critical shortage of essential drugs
and medical supplies.
3.     Ouality
2.8           Declining enrollment rates, high repetition and attrition rates, and the present low
student/teacher ratios are indicators of the education system's low quality and efficiency. The system
is adversely affected by the complex language problem, inappropriate curricula, poor preparation of
teachers, lack of instructional materials, and deficient school infrastructure. Inadequate supervision in
both the education and health systems also contributes to the low quality and inefficiency.
2.9            While unpaid community volunteers staff the basic health units at the village level,
there are insufficient numbers of trained health center personnel to staff health centers, supervise and
provide training to the community workers at the health posts. Both the teaching profession and the
health system are finding it difficult to recruit individuals because of the low salaries, lack of career
structure and incentives. The number of nursing students, for example, has significantly diminished
since 1988.
4.     Equity and Access
2.10          A combination of socioeconomic, cultural and attitudinal factors in Guinea-Bissau
affect equity and access to health and education services. Poverty is the most daunting constraint to
access. Women and girls, in particular, have limited access to health and family planning services,
education and training. Female illiteracy is greater than 80%. Only 40% of the relevant age children
attend primary school, and of this number less than 38% are girls. Of the 4.2% of the school-age
children who attend secondary school, only 36.4% are girls. While the education system does not
actively discriminate against girls and women, these figures indicate that females do not participate
equally with males in the system. Guinean society does discriminate against women in a number of
ways: there is a low value assigned to education by parents; early marriage and motherhood is the
norm; and low school enrollments and attendance rates probably reflect the low relevance of the



- 4 -
curriculum, as well as the fact that children, especially girls, are taken out of school to help with
household chores and the care of younger siblings. Poor health care and personal hygiene, which
contribute to high maternal mortality and morbidity, also indicate the severe lack of access to
information by women. Women's access to basic health services, particularly in rural areas, is
affected by a shortage of basic village health posts, trained health workers and birth attendants, and
by an over-concentration of health resources in urban areas. The number of nurses and auxiliary
midwives is less than 50% of the established ratios in almost all areas of the country, except for
Bissau, which has an excess of nurses by nearly 180% of the established ratios.
5.     Sectoral Financing
2.11          The proportion of the central budget allocated to the health sector declined from 7.6%
in 1986 to about 7% in 1990, and as a share of GDP the health sector declined from 1.6% in 1986 to
about 1.0% in 1990. The education sector's share of the central budget declined from 13.8% in 1986
to 12.9% in 1990, and as a share of GDP it declined from 2.5% in 1986 to 1.6% in 1989. These
sectoral allocations are among the lowest in the region. Investment expenditures in the social sectors
are inadequately funded and not well organized. In both the health and education sectors, what
currently serves as the Public Investment Program is basically an expenditure program, related to
donor-supported projects, which, in fact, has a high recurrent expenditure content and is heavily
dependent on external assistance. It is a program through which donor assistance, mainly in the form
of grants, is used mostly to finance recurrent costs. Financing for the social sectors, in general, is
largely dependent on foreign donor assistance. In 1989, for example, nearly 94% of "investment
expenditures" for the health sector were financed by external assistance, and 76% of these
expenditures went to finance recurrent costs of salaries, non-durable goods and services. In the
education sector approximately 99% of "investment expenditures" were externally financed and
almost 86% of these expenditures were recurrent in nature. In sum, both the health and education
sectors are highly dependent on donor assistance, without which they could not function.
C.     Health Sector
1.     Health and Nutrition Status
2.12          The health status of the population is characterized by high rates of mortality and
morbidity. Infant mortality is officially 140 per 1,000 live births, but may be closer to 180 per 1,000.
Maternal mortality is estimated at 8 per 1,000 but could also be considerably higher. The official
crude death rate is 17.7 per 1,000 inhabitants, and over 30% of this total occurs during the first year
of life. Malaria, the main cause of mortality, is also the morbidity leader and during 1989 constituted
70% of all transmittable diseases. Moderate protein-energy malnutrition, diarrhea, respiratory
diseases and sexually transmitted diseases complete the group of other leading causes of morbidity.
Approximately 80% of all births occur in the home without the assistance of any trained health
worker or community midwife. In addition, less than 20% of the population has tap water, and
almost all the rural population obtain water from unprotected, easily contaminated natural sources.
Sociocultural and environmental factors interact to increase the vulnerability of children and of
women when pregnant, in labor or nursing.
2.13          Nutrition, Various nutrition studies conducted in the 1980s indicate an average
prevalence of 40% for moderate protein energy malnutrition (PEM) and 2% for severe. Problems of
food availability, in terms of quantity and quality, lack of appropriate weaning foods, cultural taboos,
and insufficient knowledge of nutrition may be some of the contributing factors to poor diets,
especially for children under five years and pregnant and lactating women. Malnutrition in the



-5 -
country also may be more directly linked to exposure to disease and housing occupational patterns.
While health center staff know and recognize the importance of growth monitoring, they do not
organize themselves to practice it efficiently. In-service training focusing on growth monitoring, the
appropriate use of local foods, correct eating practices for pregnant and lactating women, and the
preparation of nutritionally rich weaning foods using local foods, combined with better dissemination
of health and nutrition information in general could improve the situation.
2.14          Pharmaceuticals.  An essential drugs program (EDP) technically began in 1984.
During Phase 1 (1984-89) the objectives were to: ensure availability of essential drugs in primary
health care; encourage the rational use of drugs; improve drug management throughout the system;
and create public awareness of improved drug use. The program seeks to supply the most needed
drugs, related to treating high prevalence diseases such as malaria, diarrhea, STDs and respiratory
infections which account for 90% of the demand on the health system. The EDP's yearly budget is
supposed to be about US$1.8 million, of which 83% is allocated for drug purchases and the
remainder for distribution and recurrent costs. Due to a severe lack of Government funds and foreign
exchange needed to purchase drugs (less than 10% of the drugs are procured directly using
Governmental funds) external support for the program has been necessary. The principal funding
organizations have been UNICEF, WHO, IDA, the Italian Government and the Swedish International
Development Authority. During Phase II EDP's focus has been directed to implementing a gradual
self-financing plan, based on some of the main objectives adopted from the 1987 Bamako Initiative,
which has the major objective to strengthen the health care system and includes the following
elements: (a) ensuring a self-sufficient system by involving communities in the decision-making
process in implementation activities and in the cost recovery system; (b) improving the quality of
health services and extending coverage by increasing the effectiveness of health care at the various
levels of the system; (c) ensuring a constant supply of essential drugs and their rational prescription
and use; and, (d) increasing the responsibilities of staff at the district and sub-district levels in the
planning, management and implementation of health services by decentralizing the health structure. A
pilot cost recovery program using the Bamako Initiative approach was introduced in 1990 in the Gabu
Region, in an effort to enhance the health services through community participation, whereby partial
drugs costs are recovered at the health centers (HCs) and sectoral hospitals and a percentage of these
funds are returned to the local communities. There are plans for this pilot program to be expanded to
other regions. This will happen in conjunction with the ongoing efforts to decentralize at the central
level and strengthen regional health system management, which will be supported by the proposed
IDA project through training and construction of regional drug storage facilities.
2.15          Drug storage and distribution to public health facilities are managed by the Ministry's
Central Medical Store (CMS). Distribution is on a trimester basis, and while quantities are
insufficient to cover the needs, the deliveries are made without excessive delays. The CMS drug
system management was strengthened and improvements made in the selection, procurement, storage,
distribution, prescription and utilization of drugs, and improvements in the inventory control
procedures, as a partial result of support received from the first IDA project in the health sector
through provisions of staff training, transportation and a computer for the CMS, as well as the
production and distribution of a National Drug Formulary. In addition to the CMS, there is Farmedi,
a parastatal agency which procures drugs and pharmaceutical products for various ministries and the
commercial market. Until recently Farmedi had a near monopoly on the importation of drugs.
However, with the transition to a market economy there are now a number of private pharmacies in
the country which are importing their own supplies directly, and Farmedi's role has decreased
significantly due, in part, to increased competition, the diminished ability to obtain hard currency
from the Central Bank, and also due to high recurrent and fLxed costs including personnel, in relation
to the 35% profit margin imposed by the Ministry of Commerce.



- 6 -
2.16          The processes of selection, procurement, storage and distribution of essential drugs at
the various levels are treated in the pharmaceutical policy. While legislation has been formulated and
approved by the Council of Ministers, and a National Formulary distributed, the enforcement of these
regulations is difficult for several reasons, according to the 1992 UNICEF Bamako Initiative in
Guinea-Bissau report, including:
--inadequate infrastructure to guarantee the registration of drugs and the inspection of
the facilities at which they are sold;
-lack of qualified personnel to guarantee follow-up, and a lack of a national quality
control laboratory to guarantee drugs analysis; and,
-the inability and difficulty in applying drug import regulations and customs control,
including WHO's Certification Scheme on the Quality of Pharmaceutical Products
moving in International Commerce.
Due to the new economic policies in the country it can be assumed that the newly emerging private
sector will play an increasingly important role in the pharmaceutical field, however, it may be
difficult to control and could adversely affect the implementation of essential drugs concepts and
related activities. Guinea-Bissau has, in fact, made considerable progress in trade liberalization under
SAC II. Pharmaceuticals can be freely and directly imported into the country. While they are taxed,
they incur less than one-half the average taxation. However, donors and aid-assistance for medicines
are not subject to any taxes or surcharges.
2.     Health System: Organizational Structure
2.17          The health system is controlled almost exclusively by the public sector through the
Ministry of Public Health (MINPH). MINPH is responsible for developing health policies,
organizing health-related human and physical resources, and delivering health care through a
pyramidal structure that includes: national, regional and sectoral hospitals; health centers (HCs) and
basic health units (USBs). Personnel and other health resources are controlled centrally and in some
instances administered regionally. (Annex 2-2 provides an Organization Chart of MINPH). All
MINPH resources are allocated to the delivery level according to their availability and the needs and
functioning of the service delivery points. MINPH is in the process of decentralizing health services
and delegating increased authority to the regions. Significant external assistance is received from
international organizations and non-government organizations (NGOs) for funding, personnel,
equipment and supplies. Various NGOs also assist in the operation of facilities throughout the
system. While there is no organized private sector health care system, with the Government's
increasing support for promoting privatization in the economy, a number of physicians now have
limited private practices. There are also a number of traditional healers and birth attendants. In
addition, the number of pharmacies operating in the private sector continue to grow. The type and
number of health facilities in the country in 1992, including the 25 USBs to be constructed in the
region of Biombo by 1995, are as follows: (A distribution of health facilities by Region is given in
Annex 2-3):



-7-
Type of Facility                   Number
National Hospitals:                 2
- Hospital Simao Mendes
- Hospital Tres de Agosto
Regional Hospitals                  5
Sector Hospitals                   13
Health Centers (HCs)               115
Basic Health Units (USBs)         569
2.18          Basic Health Units (USBs). The USBs, built by the communities and staffed with
volunteer basic health agents (ASBs) and traditional birth attendants or matronas, are the first delivery
level of primary health care (PHC). Each USB is designed to serve approximately 1,000 people
within a five-mile radius and provide first aid, prenatal care, and diagnosis for simple treatment of
common ailments. The number of USBs varies considerably within regions. Actual coverage is less
than half the potential coverage. For start-up purposes each USB receives an initial supply of 12
basic drugs from MINPH. Each family in the catchment area pays a small annual fee to replenish
this supply of drugs. USB staff receive limited training and supervision from MINPH personnel at
the health centers, which are the first referral level. As community based, supported and operated
health facilities, the USBs are pivotal to the Ministry's strategy for expanding PHC delivery.
2.19          Health Centers (HCs). In the service delivery system the HCs receive direction from
the regional and sectoral hospitals and, in turn, oversee the USBs by providing training and
supervision. Structurally HCs are similar in design and size. They are provided with basic
equipment and some essential drugs by MINPH or donors. Ideally HCs are staffed with two nurses,
an auxiliary midwife and a laboratory assistant. The HCs carry out some health education and
immunization activities in addition to diagnosis and prescription for malaria, infectious diseases and
minor injuries. Some are equipped with beds and offer maternity services. Each center is supposed
to serve about 5,000 persons. Actual coverage by the HCs is less than one-third the minimum
estimated needs of the rural population. Guarantee of referral and periodic supervision are key to
PHC efficiency, and central to the role of HCs in the present system. The numbers of HCs,
however, are insufficient and those that are functioning are inadequately equipped and staffed.
Auxiliary nurses are the only staff category that presently conforms to established staffing ratios.
2.20          The primary bottleneck in the whole PHC delivery system is at the bealth center level,
which has the critical functions of supervision and providing training for the USB community based
workers-ASBs and matronas-who are not professionals but individuals from the community,
frequently illiterate. The training they receive is designed to enable them to return to their
communities and perform several health-related activities and simple interventions. While these
interventions are simple, they are critically important because they impact directly on the most
significant morbidity/mortality factors: the lack of maternity assistance and maternal and child health
(MHC) services. Due to insufficient human resources, managerial capacity, basic equipment and
supplies, and transportation, which are the fundamental links in the PHC system, the HCs are not
adequately fulfilling their supervisory and training functions for the USBs, without which PHC loses
its effectiveness and is seriously distorted. This scenario contributes to an under-utilization of the
system and perpetuates the country's poor health status.



- 8 -
2.21          Hospital Services. Guinea-Bissau has 20 hospitals (2 national, 5 regional and 13
se.toral), and, in 1991, there were a total of about 1,300 hospital beds, a ratio of one bed for every
760 persons based on UN 1990 population estimates of 987,000. The sector hospitals are the smallest
of the hospitals, averaging 25 beds, and serve as a transition point between the HCs and the hospital
sector. Their inpatient care is primarily obstetrics-related. A number of these "sector hospitals",
however, are operating essentially at the health center level. The four regional hospitals, with an
average of 60 beds each, vary widely in the level of services provided. Only one, the Cacheu
Hospital in Canchungo, is equipped to offer comprehensive health care, although all are supposed to
provide services in general medicine, obstetrics, pediatrics, minor surgery, radiology, laboratory
analysis and pharmaceuticals. The hospital in Canchungo, originally built and staffed by the People's
Republic of China, is now operated by the Taiwanese with a number of capable expatriate medical
staff. The regional hospitals are also often the site for the regional directories, small units with a core
staff consisting of a regional director, a chief nurse, and a statistician responsible for administration of
PHC services in each region.
2.22          The two national hospitals are located in Bissau, and together account for almost half
the total number of doctors and certified nurses in the country. Even with this concentration,
however, hospital staffing is inadequate for the provision of tertiary care required. Both hospitals are
in poor physical condition. Simao Mendes Hospital, with about 411 beds, is the largest health facility
in the country and is supposed to focus on specialty services, however, because of the inadequacy of
health centers in Bissau it ends up providing primary health care to much of the population. The
most complete laboratory and main pharmacy and drug supply center are located at Tres Agosto
Hospital. The original structure of Tres Agosto, however, is badly deteriorated and is no longer in
use, although smaller satellite buildings are still functioning.
2.23          Other Health Services. In addition to the USBs, HCs and the network of hospitals
there are several types of specialized centers: maternal child health/family planning (MCH/FP);
mental health center; physical rehabilitation; tuberculosis center; and leprosy treatment center which
receives assistance from Italy. There is also a Center for Tropical Medicine in Bissau, supported by
Portuguese technical assistance.
3.     Human Resources
2.24          Trained health workers are vital to the delivery of health services and the
corresponding improvement of the country's health indicators. There is growing concern about both
the quantity and quality of available human resources, as well as the maldistribution of existing
personnel. The types of practicing health personnel in Guinea-Bissau include: physicians,
pharmacists, certified nurses, nurses' aides, auxiliary midwives, and laboratory assistants. In addition
to a shortage of nurses and auxiliary midwives in all regions of the country except for Bissau, the
distribution of physicians and other health personnel is also disproportionate. In 1990 there were 128
physicians in the country, 54 of whom were Guineans. This is a ratio of 1 physician per 7,711
persons, based on 1990 UN population estimates. The capital city has 1 physician per 2,450.
National physicians work mainly in hospitals. The country depends on expatriate physicians, who
account for about 58% of the total, to help meet medical manpower needs and to staff health
facilities. Recent data suggest, however, that the number of expatriate physicians is beginning to
decline. There is also concern about whether physicians from some countries, including Cuba and the
former USSR, will continue to maintain their long-term presence in the country. Until recently no
training capacity existed for personnel above the certified nurse level. A Cuban-supported non-
traditional school of medicine began operating in 1987 to prepare Guinean physicians. It graduated
its first class of about 20 students in 1992. There is uncertainty, however, about whether future
_  --  ------



9-
graduates of the Medical School, whose education emphasizes PHC, will be able to fill some of the
vacancies of expatriate physicians, many of whom are medical specialists.
2.25         Ministry data for 1992 indicate that there were 14 midwives and 143 auxiliary
midwives. In order to strengthen birth attendance and increase the number of midwives in rural
areas, MINPH has an in-service training program that "re-cycles" auxiliary nurses into auxiliary
midwives. This retraining program is supposed to reduce the excessive number of nursing auxiliaries
by redeploying them to areas of greatest need. In 1992 there were 320 graduate nurses and 1,053
auxiliary nurses. The ratio of nurses to the population is 1 nurse per 3,063 persons, and there is
growing concern about the inadequate number of nurses and the increased demand for nursing
services. The number of nurses and auxiliary midwives is less than 50% of the established ratios in
almost all areas of the country except Bissau which has an excess of nurses by nearly 180% of the
estimated ratios. In 1992 there were 6 laboratory technicians and 178 auxiliary laboratory technicians
in the country assigned to hospitals and HCs. The ASBs and matronas, who serve primarily in rural
areas as staff for the USBs, are crucial in delivering primary health care. In 1988, however, services
and consultations delivered through USBs fell far short of what they might have been: 31 % of the
expected childbirth consultations; 38% of expected prenatal consultations; and 58% of the expected
number of ASB consultations for such health problems as malaria, diarrhea, conjunctivitis, cough,
wounds, and other pain.
2.26         Training. Until recently, no training capacity existed for personnel above the certified
nurse level. Prior to the opening of the Cuban-supported six-year medical school, most Guinean
physicians were trained outside the country, depending on limited scholarships and frequently having
to learn another language in order to pursue their studies. Many of those receiving training abroad,
particularly when in Portugal, have not returned to Guinea-Bissau to practice medicine. The National
Medical School (NMS) is at a site adjacent to Simao Mendes Hospital in Bissau, and shares
administrative offices and classrooms with the Technical School. About 90% of the faculty are
Cuban. Most funding has been provided by the Cuban Government; WHO has also contributed funds
for faculty and student stipends. The training's focus is on community health problems. Students
take their clinical courses in the wards and departments at the hospital. The NMS operates in a small
multi-purpose facility with few resources. Offices and classrooms are small, poorly lighted,
ventilated and equipped. All textbooks, which are provided on loan to each class, are in Spanish.
2.27         The Technical School for Health Personnel in Bissau trains health workers including:
nurses; nursing aides, midwives; and laboratory assistants. The school is situated in a small building
on the Simao Mendes Hospital complex, and some facilities and laboratories are shared with the
NMS. The School is now funded by MINPH, NGOs and donors. The African Development Bank
financed the construction of a new building for the Technical School, which has not yet been opened.
Recruitment of nursing students has not been very successful in recent years due in part to the
profession's low image resulting from small stipends paid to students, low salaries, difficult hours and
lack of career development.
4.    Health Information. Education and Communications
2.28         MINPH's Division of Health Education is responsible for information, education and
communications (IEC) activities for the sector and, with financial and technical support from donors
and NGOs, produces and distributes educational materials for PHC workers and the general
population. A full-time technical assistant, provided by CECI, a Canadian NGO, is presently
working with the Health Education Division and providing training for the Guinean staff. Several
years ago serial albums, with Canadian NGO assistance, were produced on MCH and hygiene in the



- 10 -
tabancas, containing messages about transmissible diseases. These albums were distributed
throughout the country and have been effectively used at the USB level. The division also produces
materials, mainly posters, on hygiene, breastfeeding, family planning, malaria, etc. The development
of audio-visual materials is being supported by donor technical assistance. The Ministry is also
implementing an IEC program for AIDS, which it plans to extend to other health projects with a more
comprehensive and integrated use of the resources, including the Health Learning Materials (HLM)
Program/(Material Educativo Para Saude) (MEPS), being implemented by UNDP and WHO in
several countries. As part of this program a Center of Communication and Production of Education
Materials for Health (CECOMES), is being financed by EC under the AIDS program, which will
involve the production of a variety of audio-visual materials for health personnel training, and
population and health education. While CECOMES is not connected to MINPH's Health Education
Division, the Division may participate and coordinate in some of CECOMES' activities. UNICEF
has developed several IEC programs including community mobilization campaigns for immunization.
UNICEF also has radio programs delivered in Crioulo, Fula, Mandinga and other local languages
which include messages on immunization, nutrition and child health.
2.29         While there are a number of excellent IEC activities presently underway, they are
largely uncoordinated efforts, with the end result being an inefficient use of available resources and
diverse messages being communicated. IEC requires a well structured management system, without
the parallels, duplication and overlapping functions, as now exist. At the present, the Ministry's
Health Education Division is too weak and underfunded to assume a central coordination role for IEC
activities.
D.    Government Strategy for the Health Sector
2.30         Since 1976 Guinea-Bissau has had a policy which considers primary health care to be
the ideal strategy for reaching the rural population and extending health services to the most
vulnerable and under-served groups. The National Health Policy (NHP) was adopted, with this
underlying tenet, in 1978, and Guinea-Bissau became one of the first countries in the world to adopt
the Alma Ata Declaration. The NHP went through a series of minor modifications as new or
expanded health care delivery programs were implemented (e.g., family planning (FP) integrated with
maternal and child health, expanded immunization program, and the leprosy and tuberculosis
program). The policy was later modified to include four priority areas of focus: PHC; the essential
drug supply system; health manpower; and rehabilitation of health facilities.
2.31         MINPH's National Health Plan for 1989-95 has the objective of providing PHC for
the whole population by the year 2000. The Ministry's Plan of Action for the period 1990-92 has
focused on health system development at the central level and in three regions--Bafata, Biombo and
Oio. The Plan of Action is well targeted in its emphasis on the utilization of all available health care
resources in the country (NGOs, private and traditional health systems) and increasing the managerial
and operational capacities of the existing public health system, initially in the three selected regions.
Specifically, this includes: (a) reorientation and training of health workers in each region; (b) repair
and renovation of existing facilities and equipment, including a maintenance and repair system; (c)
institutionalization of a cost-recovery system for essential drugs; (d) integration of the AIDS control
program into the PHC program; and (e) formulation and implementation of priority programs for
nutrition, malaria and schistosomiasis. In addition to the support required for the three regions,
MINPH has designated two other areas as requiring urgent attention: (i) meeting the critical need for
essential drugs throughout the health services, and (ii) ensuring continuing education of existing health
staff and strengthening the basic medical and nursing curriculum during 1991-94. In order to provide



- 11 -
adequate and timely support to the regions, the Ministry also intends to improve organization and
management at the central level.
2.32         The Government has developed, with assistance from WHO, a health policy
implementation plan. A draft of the plan was discussed with IDA during negotiations. Following
negotiations, Government revised the plan and on January 28, 1993 issued a plan that provides an
operational schedule for implementing the health policy in Guinea-Bissau over the next several years.
It contains a financial plan integrating cost recovery measures which have already been initiated in the
Gabu region. The health policy implementation plan is found in Annex 24.
E.    Bank Group Strategy
2.33         The Bank's overall country strategy emphasizes the need to urgently address and
improve the social indicators through supporting sectoral policies to alleviate poverty and by focusing
public expenditures on improving both the efficiency and the delivery of health and education
services. The concentration of the Bank's strategy is on assisting the Government in providing the
population, particularly the most vulnerable groups, with better quality and access to social services,
especially for primary health care and basic education, and continuing to improve the development of
the country's human resource base.
F.    Bank Group Operations: Status and Lessons Learned
2.34         The Bank Group's involvement in the social sectors in Guinea-Bissau is relatively
recent. It began with the Population, Health and Nutrition (PHN) Project (Cr. 1800-GUB, approved
for US$4.2 million equivalent in May 1987, and closed in December 1991). This project's objectives
were to improve the institutional capabilities of the Ministry of Public Health in planning,
management and finance, and to strengthen the delivery of health and family planning services at the
periphery. The second IDA intervention in the social sectors was with the ongoing Basic Education
Development Project (Cr. 1914-GUB, approved for US$4.3 million equivalent in May 1988, and
scheduled to close in 1995), which aims to improve primary education and build institutional capacity
in the sector. This project, which has experienced considerable implementation problems, is in the
process of being completely reviewed by IDA and the Government. The ongoing multi-sector Social
and Infrastructure Relief Project (SIRP) (Cr. 2020-GUB, approved for US$5.0 million equivalent in
1989, and scheduled to close in June 1993) has health and education components which build on the
first two projects in providing essential inputs and improving physical structures. These projects have
given IDA the experience to act as a catalyst in helping coordinate donor involvement, influencing
sector policies and supporting institutional development in the sectors.
2.35         As the first IDA operation in the social sectors, the PHN Project, in retrospect, was
undoubtedly too conceptually ambitious in its attempt to address a wide array of problems affecting
the health sector, particularly in light of the limited capacities of the country. In a number of areas it
failed to meet the original project objectives. The project did, however, develop some appropriate
frameworks for future efforts which should have lasting effects on the health sector including:
beginning the institutionalizing of a policy and practice of cost recovery in the sector, initiating a
model for supervising rural health services which has contributed to strengthening the regional health
systems management process, improving drug system management, and undertaking policy studies in
family planning and nutrition, as well as the first demographic health survey in the country which will
contribute to strengthening the health data base. At its close the PHN project was completely
disbursed. Typically projects in the social sectors have experienced problems resulting from limited
implementation capabilities and insufficient local funds, as well as lack of coordination among donors.



- 12 -
were directly related to the country's limited human resource capacity, weak institutions and
management, and a scarcity of required counterpart funds. In addition the performance of technical
assistance in the project, which did not adequately include national counterparts or technology
transfer, was mixed. These problems negatively impacted most notably on areas related to civil
works, the restructuring of the Ministry, and instituting a viable rolling financial plan.
2.36          The experience of IDA in the social sectors in Guinea-Bissau points to designing
simpler projects that are not overly ambitious in trying to correct everything that is wrong in the
system all at once. A more concentrated approach with limited and achievable objectives is required.
The design of projects must consider the context of Guinea-Bissau's realities in terms of generally
weak local institutions affected by both the limitations of human and financial resources. The design
of social sector projects in Guinea-Bissau should also give adequate consideration to appropriate
technical assistance, which does not operate in isolation but integrally provides for technology and
knowledge transfer. National counterparts should be involved in the entire process, and receive
adequate training from expatriate technical assistants so they can eventually perform in their absence.
Institutional strengthening should also be targeted where needed. Further, coordination and
collaboration among external donors is extremely important and should be an integral part in the
design of all social sector projects. Many social sector projects can be enhanced by involving NGOs
and other donors who have direct experience in developing and targeting programs for the poor and at
the grassroots level. The design of the Social Sector Project has been based on the lessons learned
and considers the realities of Guinea-Bissau's context as it exists today.



- 13 -
III. THE PROJECT
A.    Project Objectives
3.1          The main objective of the proposed Social Sector Project is to assist Government in
improving the delivery capacity and quality of primary health care for a wider base of the population,
with a special focus on the most vulnerable groups, particularly those at the periphery in rural areas
of the country. The project would achieve this objective by financing high priority investments,
through coordinating efforts with donor agencies, targeted institutional strengthening and supporting
human capacity building measures. Specifically, the project would seek to improve health and social
indicators through (a) simultaneously strengthening selective institutions at the central level of
MINPH, including support for the Ministry's regional decentralization efforts in progress for PHC,
and (b) capitalize on the proven effectiveness and strengths of NGOs and grassroots organizations
through support for a Social Action Fund. The project is designed to enhance dialogue and create
synergy in social sector institutions and within the donor and NGO communities in the country. In
health, the project would finance the rehabilitating and equipping of selected health centers and other
health facilities, including the construction of houses for nurses, in three regions and Bissau, as well
as rehabilitating health posts throughout the country using community labor; comprehensive support
for in-service, local and short-term training programs for all levels of health workers and
professionals; and technical assistance to coordinate information, education and communications (IEC)
activities, develop an IEC policy framework, and suggest targeted programs to better disseminate
health messages. The project would link the social sectors and support local and international NGOs
through the establishment of a Social Action Fund, which would be situated within an existing
umbrella institution that coordinates activities of NGOs. This fund, targeted to benefit some of the
most vulnerable groups and alleviate poverty, could be tapped by those NGOs submitting qualifying
proposals to expand or develop micro-social oriented projects, such as income generating activities for
women, nutrition programs, family planning activities, literacy, numeracy and skills development,
throughout the country. (An organigram combining a Project Coordination Unit and a Social
Action Fund Unit, which serve as administrative entities in coordinating activities related to
achieving the project's objectives is found in Annex 3-1).
B.    Proiect ComDosition
3.2          In sum, the proposed project would consist of the following sub-projects in the health
sector and a social action fund to support NGOs in developing cross-sectoral micro-social projects:
Part I. Health Sector Subprojects
A. Comprehensive Training for Health Workers and Professionals Subproject
B. Information, Education and Communications Subproject
C. Improvement of Selected Health Facilities and Community Participation Program
Subproject
Part II. Social Action Fund Subproject
A. Creation of Social Action Fund to Support NGOs in Micro-Projects for
Population, Health and Nutrition; Education and Training; and Women's
Development



- 14 -
C.    Rationale for Subproiect Selection
3.3           The selection of the subprojects included in the proposed Social Sector Project was
based on the findings and recommendations of the Bank's social sector review mission, carried out in
October/November 1990, which resulted in a Social Sectors Strategy Review (issued in green cover in
September 1991). The main issues identified by the sector review are discussed in the previous
chapter. The proposed subprojects are also consistent with the Government's priorities in the social
sectors. Further, the selection of the subprojects builds on the foundation created by the earlier IDA
interventions in the country's social sectors: the Population, Health and Nutrition Project, which
closed in December 1991; the on-going Basic Education Development Project, which is presently
being redesigned; and the Social and Infrastructure Relief Project. Experience gained from these
projects indicates a need to focus efforts, simplify project design, and ensure that an investment
operation in the social sectors is capable of being implemented within the context of the country's
realities and its limited human resource base.
3.4           The social sectors review made recommendations for the following priority areas: the
development of human resources; rehabilitation of existing facilities; provision of essential inputs; and
the development of a coordinated information, education and communications program. In the health
sector, the proposed project will focus on comprehensive training programs for all levels of health
care workers; rationalizing and improving the health infrastructure by rehabilitating selected health
facilities and supporting community efforts with inputs at the basic health posts level; and, technical
assistance to study how better to coordinate IEC activities and suggest a policy framework, equipment
and inputs to strengthen the Ministry's Health Education Division, and support for targeted IEC
programs in areas not now well covered such as nutrition, family planning, and women's issues, as
well as AIDS and endemic diseases. The proposed project would also provide funds for the
establishment of a Social Action Fund to support the expansion and/or creation of cross-sectoral social
sector projects developed by local and international NGOs at the micro-project level in order to reach
the most vulnerable segments of the population, particularly in rural villages.
D.    Project Description
Health Sector SubDroiects
1.    Comprehensive Training for Health Workers Subproject
3.5            A comprehensive and continuous training process, that has an out-reach approach,
has been identified as the most fundamental input required to further the development of human
resources in the health sector. The objective of this subproject is to assist the Government by
strengthening the capacity of MINPH to improve the quality and delivery of health care through a
comprehensive training program for all levels of health care workers, taking into account the existing
number of health professionals and additional needs of the country (Annex 3-21. Specifically, it
would: (a) provide professional knowledge and skills to health workers; (b) upgrade health workers
with low levels of qualification in order to better perform their tasks; and (c) provide the skills
necessary to help in carrying out the decentralization process and strengthening of the regional health
management services. The various training sub-components would cover (i) short-term in-service
training (less than six months) and long-term in-service training (more than six months); (ii) formal
education carried out locally or regionally; and (iii) multi-oriented training supported with technical
assistance. These programs would be closely linked to overcoming the poor health care status in the
country and, thereby, improving health indicators. (A detailed description of this subproject and its
implementation schedule are found in Annexes 3-3. 34. 3-5. and 3-6).



- 15 -
3.6          The benefits of the training subproject will be to: (a) improve the quality of health
care delivery through training of health workers without sufficient professional qualification, which
represents about 74% of the existing health manpower in the country; giving preference to refresher,
upgrading and specialization programs for existing health workers over new formal education
programs; assuring equal access to training for workers distributed all over the country; and supplying
equipment and diagnostic facilities in order to facilitate and improve the training of health
professionals at all levels; and (b) generally raise the overall conditions in the country in order to
develop adequate patterns of technical and professional behavior supported by physical improvements
and equipping selected health facilities. This subproject has been designed by focusing on those
training areas identified by Government and IDA to be priority, including: decentralization, drugs
management and supply, and cost recovery.
3.7          In-Service Training Refresher Programs. The in-service refresher courses will be
designed to upgrade the skills of health personnel. The main objective is to improve skills and
knowledge in the areas that were defined as priorities within the system, including decentralized
management, statistics and information systems, drug management, health financing, and
environmental sanitation. A large part of the in-service training program would be supported by the
assistance of local consultants, and conducted within the framework of existing institutions. The
specific types of training programs that have been identified and would be supported by the project
include the following:
Programns                       Number of Students

for Technicians                             25

- Introduction to Drug Management
for Auxiliary Nurses                        50
- Environmental Sanitation                    25
3.8          In-Service Training Upgrading Programs. The subproject would support several new
programs in formal health education in specialized areas, which would, combined with the in-service
training proposed above, further improve the delivery of primary health care. The main objective of
these programs is to upgrade the skills of health personnel to improve quality and effectiveness in the
system and reduce the number of unqualified personnel. These programs would mainly affect
auxiliary nurses, whose numbers presently exceed the requirements, by upgrading their qualifications
and converting them into nurses or health technicians. This would result in increased numbers of
trained health personnel filling positions where there are presently an insufficient number to meet the
needs. These programs would, generally, be carried out at the Nursing and Technical Schools. The
project would provide some equipment, supplies and learning materials to strengthen the institutions
involved in delivering the training programs. The new programs identified would include:
