3.9          In-Service Training for Medical Specialization. The primary objectives of this
component of the subproject are to support the local development of medical specializations in basic
areas and improve the specialized skills of professional medical staff necessary to provide better
quality and delivery of medical care to the population, particularly through local in-service training in
pediatrics (10 participants), obstetrics/gynecology (10 participants), general surgery (10 participants),
internal medicine (10 participants), and public health (20 participants). In addition, this component of
the subproject will support and strengthen the institutions involved in the training program, and
create a viable referral system. Bissau health centers will serve as hands-on training institutions for
primary health care, as will Simao Mendes Hospital, and will be supported through the project with
some medical and other equipment required for training doctors and other health personnel. Local
technical coordination for this component would be required by designated MINPH representatives
and WHO in Bissau.
3.10             The following areas would be supported by the project:
(a)       the Medical School through provision of books, journals and technical reviews,
providing direct support for students currently attending the Medical School;
(b)       Simao Mendes Hospital through provision of medical and other equipment
required for training doctors and other health personnel; and
(c)       foreign technical assistance to create some specialized training programs at Simao
Mendes Hospital, including: specialists to prepare/teach the local trainers; and
technical assistance to teach several Guinean doctors (who have had at least two
years of practical experience) per year in the specialties including pediatrics,
obstetrics, general surgery, and internal medicine.
2.        Information, Education and Communications (aEC) Subproiect
3.11             Guinea-Bissau's health problems cannot all be corrected in hospitals or health
centers. A multi-faceted but linked approach is required in making improvements in the quality and
delivery of basic health care. Concurrent with improving human resource development through
training programs for health care workers and revitalizing health facilities, particularly health centers,
which have been identified as the bottleneck in the primary health care system, and village health
posts, a better attempt must be made to change the behavior and attitudes of the population which are



- 17 -
incompatible with the preservation of health and prevention of diseases. This can be accomplished, in
part, through effective information, education and communications programs that reach rural
populations, in general, and women, in particular. Education for health promotion and prevention of
diseases is fundamental to the success of any program within the framework of primary health care.
3.12            Guinea-Bissau currently benefits from many varied IEC activities and support from
a number of international agencies and NGOs. However, these activities combined with national
initiatives are not well coordinated by the Government, and the dissemination of health information is
uneven. The Ministry recently proposed that a National Council for Health Information, Education
and Communications (CNIECS) be established. The following strategic actions have also been
identified by the Governrment in order to improve IEC in the country: (a) promoting training to
support IEC activities; (b) health education policy definition; (c) strengthening the health/education
division of MINPH in order to utilize it as a focal point in all health/education areas; (d) preparing
follow-up studies based on the activities carried out in health/education; (e) strengthening cooperation
and coordination among all organizations involved in delivering IEC in the country; (f) strengthening
MlNPH's Information and Documentation Center; and (g) developing teaching materials related to
health topics.
3.13            The objectives of the IEC subproject would be to assist the Government in
developing and implementing its IEC strategy and improving the coordination of IEC activities.
These improvements will have a direct effect on the Government's ability to better deliver health
education to the population, particularly to women and the most vulnerable in remote rural areas.
The following areas would be supported by the project: (i) short-term technical assistance to evaluate
the overall IEC activities in the country and assist the newly formed Council to further develop an
IEC framework within the context of the country. The TA would also evaluate the Ministry's health
education division and make recommendations as to how it can better develop adequate and useful
IEC activities as well as to serve as the country's central IEC coordinating body; (ii) training and
seminars on IEC for health personnel, and training and seminars for personnel involved in IEC
delivery in graphic arts, illustration, layout and design, and audio-visual; (iii) equipment and supplies
to strengthen the health education division and the Information and Documentation Center; and (iv)
funds to edit, adapt and distribute health information contained in two serial albums, "Health in the
Tabancas" and "Maternal and Child Health", which were produced a number of years ago by MINPH
with the support of a Canadian NGO. The materials resulting from these efforts will be made
available to all USBs and health centers in the country. (A detailed description of this subproject is
found in Annex 3-7).
3.       Strengthening Delivery of Health Services through Improvement of Health
Facilities/Community Participation Subproject
3.14            Many health facilities are in need of minor to substantial renovation. Some are in
a deplorable state and require complete replacement. A 1990 study on the health system's
infrastructure coverage reported that health facilities were adequately providing PHC to less than 65%
of the population. The Ministry plans, however, to first focus on improving and rehabilitating
existing health facilities, and construction of new ones only when the deterioration has gone beyond
the ability to repair. Similarly, many health facilities lack essential medical equipment and supplies.
In addition to repairing and equipping facilities to improve the delivery of primary health care, it is
necessary to attract qualified nurses and other trained health personnel to staff health centers in rural
areas.



- 18 -
3.15             The proposed subproject would assist the Ministry of Public Health in
rehabilitating/constructing selected health regional centers, health centers, staff houses, drug storage
units, and providing roofing and flooring materials for qualifying basic health units. The focus of the
civil works for regional centers, health centers, staff houses, and drug storage units would be initially
in the three regions of Bafata, Oio, and Biombo. Also financed would be access to safe water and
sanitation by the installation of wells at 12 health centers and the installation of solar panels at 22
health centers located in the three regions. In addition, one health center in Bissau will be repaired
under the project. This would support the Government's activities, which are underway, to
decentralize and strengthen the health management system in the three regions. (Technical details of
this subproject, including guidelines for construction of staff houses by the communities, are
found in Annex 3-8). In order to attract nurses to serve in rural areas and further strengthen the
regional management system, the subproject would provide for the construction of simple houses for
nurses adjacent to the health centers in the three regions. The communities would be encouraged to
provide some of the labor and to participate in the construction of these houses. (Detailed
description of past experience in community participation in the country is found in Annex 3-.
Appropriate incentives for community participation, such as food for work, transistor radios, and T-
shirts will be provided under the project. (Annex 3-10 provides detailed information on this
scheme).
3.16              The subproject would also provide funds to support the basic health units through
provision of simple tools and equipment for the villages to repair these structures. The subproject
would also provide subsidized corrugated zinc to replace the palm frond roofs presently on most of
these structures, and cement to replace dirt floors. To keep the community participation spirit that
exists in most of the smaller villages (tabancas) in the country, the villages with USBs would
determine if they want to have the roof replaced and would be expected to raise a small portion of the
funds required, i.e., 20%, which would go back into purchasing supplies for the village health post,
as well as contribute the labor. The works would be carried out by the local communities, which
would be responsible for undertaking construction activities following their own traditions. It is
anticipated that about 130 and 110 basic health units will request roofing and flooring replacement in
the three regions, respectively. The subproject will also consider roofing and flooring replacement
requirements by those USBs in other regions of the country. (A detailed proposal from
Government on this subproject can be found in Annex 3-11).
3.17             The subproject would further support the USBs and health centers throughout the
country by providing each with a bicycle and tool repair kit. A bicycle would be an important link
between the village USBs and the health centers. Each USB and HC would also be provided under
the subproject with a transistor radio, which will better enable health education messages to reach
people in some of the more remote areas of the country.
3.18             In sum, the health facilities improvement subproject would include the following
elements:
(a)       upgrading, rehabilitating, repairing, equipping, furnishing and maintaining about
10 HCs;
(b)       constructing, rehabilitating, furnishing, equipping and maintaining two health
regional centers, 29 houses for nurses, and three drug storage units (a list of
equipment and furniture is found in Annex 3-12);



- 19 -
(c)      providing roofing and flooring materials and furnishing to about 240 USBs in the
three regions, and reserve funds for USBs elsewhere in the country which submit
requests and are able to meet the requirements for these materials;
(d)      installing wells at 12 HCs and solar panels at 22 HCs;
(e)      providing 600 bicycles and tool repair kits for USBs and HCs throughout the
country, transistor radios for all USBs and workers participating in the
construction of staff houses; and,
(f)      providing vehicles including pick-up trucks, ambulances, and motorcycles to the
three regions.
Social Action Fund Subproject
3.19            In February 1992 the Government approved and recognized the legal position of
NGOs and formulated a law (decreto Lei dos ONG's Nacionais) that regulates national NGOs. This
was the official recognition of a process that had started in the mid-1980s and that has gained
considerable momentum ever since. In early 1992 there were 12 national NGOs and 63 international
NGOs registered and actively providing subsidies and technical assistance to more than 100 projects
in Guinea-Bissau. More than 40 of these projects were targeting assistance for the social sectors.
NGOs in Guinea-Bissau have proven to have a good track record for the development of successful
small-scale social sector projects which have the ability to quickly reach and have an impact on some
of the most vulnerable groups in the society. The rapid growth and participation of NGOs in the
socio-economic development process (from 20 in 1982 to 75 in 1992) led the country to establish
SOLIDAMI (SOLIdariedade e AMIzade) in 1984. This organization was created as a public
institution under the auspices of the Ministry of Planning and International Cooperation. In 1986 it
became financially and administratively independent from the Government, while retaining an
organizational link with the Ministry of Planning. The organizational link is now with the recently
established Ministry of Foreign Affairs and Cooperation (MINFAC). SOLIDAMI, which is
supported by a number of donors, is composed of four departments that promote, support, document
and administer the various projects of NGOs in the country. SOLIDAMI's main objectives are to act
as an umbrella to coordinate NGO activities in the country, support the creation and development of
national NGOs, and support small local initiatives from grassroots organizations in different regions
and sectors, particularly in the areas of production and credit. No specific objectives for the social
sectors have been formulated under SOLIDAMI although a substantial number of NGOs are active in
the field. SOLIDAMI funds are available for only local groups and national NGOs. (A list of
international and local NGOs in Guinea-Bissau is found in Annex 3-13.)
3.20            NGOs in Guinea-Bissau have the ability, and a proven track record, to cut through
the country's diverse cultural patterns and multi-language complexities through the promotion of
small-scale projects to improve the conditions of some of the country's most disadvantaged. Their
outreach experience at the grassroots level will be capitalized on through the proposed project, with
the ultimate objective being the measurable improvement of health and social indicators, and
concomitantly the quality of life. In order to better support and expand the activities of NGOs in the
social sectors designed to alleviate poverty of the society's most vulnerable groups, a Social Action
Fund (SAF) has been established with enough flexibility to permit support for a variety of small-scale
projects addressing social problems in various regions of the country. (A detailed description of this
subproject with terms of reference of key technical assistance is found in Annex 3-14.)
Specifically, the objective of this fund, supported through the subproject, is to allow eligible NGOs,



- 20 -
and eventually qualifying grassroots groups and associations, to draw upon a source of additional
resources in order to expand or develop micro-projects for targeted social programs. The most
important beneficiaries of the SAF will be rural and urban households below the poverty-line, which
are unable to assure sufficient revenue for their basic needs nor the access to essential health and
education services. Among some of the specific targeted groups to be reached are women
(particularly those heading single households, widows, prostitutes); children (particularly orphans,
street children, juvenile delinquents); elderly people without family support; and handicapped persons.
The type of micro-projects that will be eligible for funding by the SAF include income generating
activities for women, targeted health, nutrition and family planning programs, skills training, and
literacy and numeracy programs, among others. Efforts to enhance collaboration between national
and international NGOs, such as developing a "twinning arrangement" will be considered positively
and encouraged. Such an arrangement will allow local NGOs to benefit from the experience of
international NGOs and develop the necessary organizational, managerial and administrative skills
required for project development and implementation.
3.21             Expatriate technical assistance and a local consultant, both with significant
experience in working with SOLIDAMI and NGOs in Guinea-Bissau, were contracted under the
Project Preparation Facility (PPF) to assist Government in developing a framework for the SAF
including specific project criteria and guidelines for qualifying project proposals, and setting the scope
and parameters of the NGO micro-projects that will be eligible for funding under the SAF (para
3.20). The funding mechanism for administering the SAF was also elaborated. The resulting
product: a detailed operational framework to implement the SAF was submitted by Government to
IDA at negotiations, and the main features of the Fund were agreed to during negotiations. The
following general criteria will apply when submitting proposals to be financed by the SAF:
(a)   proposals should be linked to social problems and poverty alleviation;
(b)   proposals should be supportive to high risk groups in the society that are directly
affected by the economic reform programs;
(c)   proposals should be directed to groups of people that show interest and initiative and
are inclined to foster community participation;
(d)   proposals cannot be submitted nor will funds be distributed to those groups that
already have a privileged position in the country; and
(e)   proposals cannot exceed a maximum of US$15,000 per project.
3.22         Through the SAF the subproject would accomplish the following: (a) the delivery of
various social services to dispersed areas of the country through cross sectoral micro social projects;
(b) the training and development of local NGOs; and (c) providing a funding mechanism for NGOs to
expand some of their successful projects and to develop new ones. The SAF is an innovative first
attempt in Guinea-Bissau to tap the expertise of international and national NGOs in the country, which
are well positioned to target outreach programs at the grassroots level, to assist some of the poorest
and most disadvantaged individuals in the society through small-scale social programs. Further, the
proposed subproject will assist in the development and institutional strengthening of local Guinean
NGOs, and eventually grassroots organizations and groups, through their affiliation with the more
experienced international counterparts.



- 21 -
3.23         The SAF was officiallyestablished by Government resolution dated January 27, 1993.
Once the SAF's credibility has been established, co-financing from interested donor agencies will also
be sought to finance the Fund. It is intended that through the combination of eventual donor
financing and the Social Action Fund's location within the organizational structure of SOLIDAMI, the
SAF will become a viable entity beyond the life of the project. Funding under the project is provided
for: (i) staff, space, supplies, and equipment to set-up the SAF; (ii) short-term technical assistance to
assist in setting up the fund; (iii) training for staff; and (iv) grants to support qualified NGOs'
proposals for micro-projects that will be reviewed and approved by the SAF Technical Committee.
3.24         A Social Action Fund Unit (SAFU) has been created within the existing structure of
SOLIDAMI. (An organigram is found in Annex 3-15.) This arrangement will allow for
maximizing coordination, exchange of experience and information, while at the same time assuring
relative autonomy in order to respond rapidly and flexibly to the needs of the target groups. The
SAFU is headed by a national Managing Director who will manage and control the funds of the SAF
as well as administer the daily activities of the Unit. A Technical Committee, established under
SAFU, will meet on a regular basis and review project proposals from NGOs. The Committee,
chaired by the SAFU Managing Director, includes technical representatives from the social sector
ministries including MINPH, Ministry of National Education (MINNE), a local NGO representative,
an international NGO representative, and SOLIDAMI representative. The national and international
NGO representatives will serve on the Technical Committee on a one-year revolving basis. Those
proposals approved by the SAF Technical Committee, along with the written agreements with the
beneficiaries, would be submitted to IDA for its review and approval. In addition to the Managing
Director the SAFU is staffed by a local Assistant to the Managing Director who will provide
assistance to the Managing Director, assist beneficiaries and local NGOs in preparing project
proposals, and visit sites where SAF financed activities are being carried out to supervise and monitor
project activities. SAFU's staff will also include an accountant, a secretary and a driver.
E.     Project Costs and Financing
3.25         Project Costs. The total cost of the project is estimated at US$9.65 million
equivalent, including US$0.42 million in duties and taxes. Out of the total project costs, 84% is
investment costs and the remaining 16% is for incremental recurrent costs. Total base cost estimates
are US$8.4 million at September 1992 prices. Physical and price contingencies are estimated at
US$1.2 million (12% of total cost) with physical contingencies representing 8% of base cost. Price
contingencies have been derived from the implementation schedule and the following expected price
increases: foreign prices 3.7% in 1994, 1995, 1996, and 1997; local prices 20% in 1994, 15% in
1995, and 10% per annum thereafter. The project would be implemented over a four-year period,
with an expected completion date of June 30, 1997. Detailed project cost estimates are given in
Annex 3-16.
3.26         Financing Plan. The total project cost of US$9.23 million equivalent, excluding duties
and taxes, covers US$7.55 million in foreign costs and US$1.68 million in local costs, is shared as
follows:
(a)   the proposed IDA credit of US$8.8 million, which will cover US$7.55 million of
foreign costs and US$1.25 million of local costs, or 95% of total project costs,
excluding taxes and duties;
(b)   the Government's share of US$0.30 million, which represents the entire costs of living
allowances for participants and travel allowances for trainers under local training,



- 22 -
salaries of support staff under the PCU and SAFU, and part of civil works, or about
3% of total costs, excluding taxes and duties;
(c)   as part of an effort to incorporate community participation and a sense of ownership in
project development, it is expected that local labor will be provided from the
communities for the construction/rehabilitation of 29 staff houses and for the
replacement of roofs and floors at USBs. The terms and conditions of the community
participation would be based on agreement reached between the communities and
Government (US$0.13 million or about 2% of total cost, excluding taxes and duties).
3.27         Recurrent Cost Implications. Given the limited physical expansion envisaged under
the project, the expected incremental recurrent costs to be directly generated by project investments
would be minimal. The only addition in construction is for one regional center in Biombo and three
small drug storage units located in each of the three regions targeted in the project. The additional
recurrent costs created by these facilities are believed to be small. The staff houses will be
maintained by the occupying staff and/or the communities as part of an agreement between the
communities and Government. Recruitment of the local PCU and SAFU staff would be undertaken
on a contractual basis for the length of the project. The impact of the in-service upgrading programs,
provided under the training component of the project, on the Government's recurrent budget for
salaries, will represent, using 1992 prices, an estimated 864 million GP in MINPH recurrent cost
expenditures (14.8% of MINPH 1992 total salary budget and 10.14% of MINPH 1992 overall
budget). However, this will be minimized to a degree, as during negotiations it was agreed that the
Government would maintain a minimum budgetary allocation to the health sector of 8% of the total
national budget and attempt to increase this base by at least 1 % per annum in 1993 and thereafter
during project implementation. Since the in-service upgrading programs will increase the skills of
about 20% of existing health manpower, and therefore raise the overall quality of delivery of health
services, it is anticipated that the long term benefit of this investment in human resources will
outweigh the incremental recurrent costs to be incurred by the Government.
IV. PROJECT IMPLEMENTATION
A.    Project Preparation
4.1           The project was prepared by the Ministry of Public Health, the Ministry of Foreign
Affairs and Cooperation, and IDA staff following discussions held during the Identification, Pre-
Appraisal and Appraisal Missions. MINPH had also participated in the earlier sector work which
resulted in the Social Sectors Strategy Review issued in green cover in September 1991, which
identified issues and facilitated the identification of the proposed project.
B.     Project Management and Coordination
4.2          The proposed Social Sector Project includes three subprojects for the health sector and
one subproject supporting NGOs in developing micro-level social programs. Primary responsibility
for the implementation of the health sector subprojects will be with the Ministry of Public Health.
Specifically, the following MINPH General Directorates will be involved: Planning/Studies and
Cooperation; Human Resources; and the primary health care division of the Public Health General
Directorate. A Project Implementation Committee, under MINPH, will consist of the Ministry of
Public Health's Director of Planning, who will chair the committee, the project's Training and
Management Technical Director, the project's procurement/architectural specialist, the Ministry's
National Director of PHC, and the PCU Director, who will serve as secretary. This committee will



- 23 -
meet regularly and will provide technical and policy guidance and facilitate the health sector
subprojects' implementation. The Project's routine administration, management and day-to-day
coordination will be done through an existing project coordinating unit (PCU), presently responsible
for executing multi-sector projects under the general oversight of the Ministry of Foreign Affairs and
Cooperation. The PCU will be headed by an experienced national Project Director, who has been the
national director for the IDA financed Social and Infrastructure Relief Project (SIRP) and has been
contracted under a PPF for the Social Sector Project. The SIRP is expected to close in June 1993,
just as start-up activities for the Social Sector Project begin to accelerate. Primary responsibility for
the implementation of the SAF subproject is with the SAFU Managing Director, with technical input
on the viability of project proposals from the SAFU Technical Committee. (An organigram of the
PCU and terms of reference of key technical assistance are found in Annexes 4-1 and 4-2,
respectively).
4.3          General coordination and administrative management of the project is to be carried out
by the Project Director. The Project Director heads a small office which includes a project
accountant and several support staff. Using the administrative framework of an existing project unit
should ease the transition and facilitate implementation of the new project. This arrangement of
building on an existing institution will also allow the new project to take full advantage of the
experience of the existing staff, including the Project Director who is familiar with IDA procurement
and disbursement procedures and would have responsibility for administrative and contractual
procedures necessary for the implementation of project activities for all subprojects. A locally
recruited Training and Management Technical Director, who will have responsibility for coordinating
and implementing the training and IEC subprojects, will be funded by the project. A national
procurement/architectural specialist, to be recruited and funded by the project, will have specific
responsibility for implementing the rehabilitation subproject. Provisions will be made as necessary
under the project for international technical assistance for a procurement/implementation specialist to
support the national specialist in connection with the rehabilitation subproject, as well as technical
assistance, as required, to support the Training and Management Technical Director in implementing
the training for medical specialists component of the training for health workers subproject.
4.4           The project would finance incremental staff salaries, vehicles, equipment and materials
necessary for operating the PCU. A competitive salary incentive scheme will be developed for PCU
staff and key staff implementing the subprojects, consisting of a yearly merit review of the staff
against measurable performance criteria based on an implementation schedule for key project
activities. Individual incremental salary increases ranging from 3-10% will be determined for the
PCU Director, the SAFU Director, the project accountant, procurement/architectural specialist, and
the Training and Management Technical Director based on the project's implementation progress.
Funds for training project staff locally in computers and basic accounting and record keeping will also
be made available. In addition, the Bissau WHO office has received a formal request from MINPH
and has agreed to assist in coordinating, monitoring and supervising the health sector subprojects. In
connection with this WHO will assist the Ministry in developing measurable indicators to monitor and
evaluate the impact of the subprojects on the country's health status. This would include developing
and conducting a simple survey periodically of health workers and beneficiaries.
4.5           The Social Action Fund (SAF) subproject is managed by a Social Action Fund Unit
(SAFU) which is located structurally within SOLIDAMI, a public institution under the auspices of
MINFAC. SAFU is headed by a Managing Director who will manage and control the funds of the
SAF and administer the daily activities of the unit. Funds will be provided to SAFU through the PCU
Director, who will be the primary link with IDA in procurement and disbursement aspects of the
project. The SAFU Managing Director is assisted by a locally hired assistant who will also assist



- 24 -
beneficiaries and national NGOs in preparing project proposals, as well as visiting sites, and
monitoring and evaluating activities financed by the SAF to ensure adequate use of the funds. An
SAF Technical Committee, headed by the SAFU Managing Director, and comprised of one
representative from each of the technical social sector ministries (MINPH and MINNE), national and
international NGOs, and a SOLIDAMI representative, will meet on a regular basis to review project
proposals submitted by NGOs and decide on actions to be taken. The national and international
representatives will serve on the SAF Technical Committee on a one-year revolving basis. The
SAFU Director will prepare a quarterly report covering all activities and issues related to the SAF
and provide this report to the PCU Director for submission to IDA with the regular quarterly reports.
C.    Project Sustainability
4.6          Several features of this project contribute to its sustainability. Most importantly is the
fact that it will build on the foundation of experience gained and lessons learned from the first two
IDA interventions in the social sectors. The Social Sector Project will be a follow-up to the first IDA
project in the health sector, which was completed in December 1991, and it will build on the on-
going Social and Infrastructure Relief Project. The proposed project will be a first effort at
developing a project which addresses specific issues in the health sector, as well as broader social
issues in Guinea-Bissau through the creation of a fund to support NGO's micro-social sector projects
at the grassroots level. The project will benefit from intensive donor coordination efforts undertaken
by IDA during the preparation of the social sector work, the February 1992 social sectors donors
meeting, and throughout the course of implementing the PHN, the SIRP, and the Basic Education
projects. Further, a yearly donor's meeting will be held to review progress in social programs. The
fact that the proposed project will have a Social Action Fund subproject, to support certain micro-
projects developed by NGOs, should serve to further enhance donor collaboration and coordination.
In addition, WHO is expected to play an important role in providing assistance to Government for
monitoring and supervising the health worker training programs. Guinea-Bissau's absorptive capacity
will be strengthened by the project's primary emphasis on developing human resources and capacity
building through large-scale sectoral training programs, combined with institutional strengthening, and
provisions for appropriate technical assistance which will be designed to require that
technology/knowledge transfer be incorporated. It is suggested that any future IDA supported
projects for the social sectors continue to focus on these specific elements, and, in particular, continue
to develop human capacity building in the country to ensure sustainability. The proposed project
additionally incorporates a number of provisions to ensure satisfactory project implementation
including the design of the project management with the formation of a Project Implementation
Committee linked to MINPH and the Project Coordinating Unit, created from an existing unit
implementing the SIRP and located in the Ministry of Foreign Affairs and Cooperation. An
experienced national Project Director will oversee project administration and coordination. Similarly,
an experienced national Mananging Director has been named to head SAFU which has been
structurally located within SOLIDAMI, an existing institution which reports to the Minister of
Planning and International Cooperation. The SAF will have a Technical Committee which includes
representation from local and international NGOs and the technical social sector ministries. There
will also be a mid-term review of the project at which time measurable indicators, to be designed by
the Government with WHO assistance, such as improvements in qualifications of health personnel, in
access, delivery and the quality of health services resulting from the planned increased numbers of
trained nurses and health workers detailed to rural areas, will be monitored and assessed. A simple
survey will also be developed and conducted periodically to measure the overall impact of the project
on improving the health situation.



- 25 -
4.7          Guinea-Bissau's ability to provide counterpart contributions for externally assisted
projects is very low at the present time. However, part of the Government's share for counterpart
funds will come from the community participation contributions of labor and materials in the
construction of civil works. For these reasons the counterpart contribution is not expected to pose
significant problems in the project's implementation.
D.    Project Monitoring and Reporting
4.8          Monitoring and reporting on the state of the project's implementation progress would
be the overall responsibility of the Project Director. The Training and Management Technical
Director, a locally recruited consultant, will be responsible for the specific implementation,
monitoring and evaluation activities related to the health workers' training and the IEC subprojects,
and the Procurement/Architectural Specialist, responsible for the specific implementation, monitoring
and evaluation activities related to the rehabilitation subproject. Both will serve on the Project
Implementation Committee, along with the Ministry of Public Health's Director of Planning, Studies
and Cooperation, and the National Director of Primary Health Care, who will provide guidance and
technical input for implementation of the subprojects. The Training Director and the procurement
specialist will work closely with the PCU Director, who will also serve on the Project Implementation
Committee as secretary and will oversee all administrative matters related to the project. They will
submit systematic written progress reports on the respective subprojects they have responsibility for
implementing to the PCU Director. The PCU Director will be responsible for preparing quarterly
reports and highlighting major issues calling for resolution by the Project Implementation Committee.
These reports would be sent to IDA at the end of each quarter, and be discussed during each IDA
supervision mission. In addition, WHO will play a major role in supervising, monitoring and
evaluating the subprojects (para 4.4). (Key performance indicators are found in Annex 4-.
E.     Mid-Term Review
4.9          IDA's experience in the social sectors in Guinea-Bissau is limited to one operation in
the health sector (the PHN project which closed in December 1991); one operation in the education
sector, the on-going Basic Education Development Project; and, health and education sub-components
in the on-going Social and Infrastructure Relief Project. It is expected that the implementation
process of the Social Sector Project will provide lessons which might prompt mid-course corrections
in the design or implementation methods of some of the subprojects. In order to allow for this
possibility, the Government of Guinea-Bissau and IDA would carry out a mid-term review of the
whole project. This would involve a critical analysis of the in-service and multi-oriented training
programs for health workers subproject, the IEC subproject, and the progress in implementing the
rehabilitation of health facilities and constructing nurses' houses with community participation
subproject. For the various programs in the training subproject, in particular, training achievements
and targets will be measured during the mid-term review and for subsequent years of the project, to
determine if there is need for adjustments in the various programs. The mid-term review would also
analyze operational aspects of the social action fund subproject, and discuss any remedial corrections
to be made with the SAF Technical Committee. The Government would carry out jointly with IDA a
mid-term review of the project approximately 18 months after credit effectiveness, but not later than
July 1995. This review should also incorporate an assessment of the project's impact (para. 4.6) and
the preliminary results of a survey of measurable indicators to improve the country's health status
(para. 4.8). An assessment of the SAF and projects supported by the Fund will aso be made at the
time of the mid-term review. Based on this review, the Government would promptly prepare and
implement an action program, acceptable to IDA, for making necessary corrective actions and
improving the project's implementation. (Supervision plan and mid-term evaluation criteria are
found in Annex 4-8).



- 26 -
F.     Environrnental AsDects
4.10          The project will have no significant environmental effects. In conjunction with the
repair and substitution of selected health facilities (at the health center level) access to potable water
and sanitary facilities, if not already existing, will be included under the project.

4.12          Civil Works. The civil works included under the project are for: (a) construction of one
health regional center in Biombo (at a cost of about US$30,000); construction of three small drug storage
units each located in Bafata, Oio, and Biombo (unit cost of about US$18,000); construction of 24 staff houses
for nurses, in the Bafata, Oio and Biombo Regions (unit cost of about US$12,000); and (b)
rehabilitation/extension of nine health centers in the three regions (unit cost of about US$38,000), and
remodeling of one health center in Bissau (at a cost of about US$12,500); extension of one health regional
center in Bafata (at a cost of about US$6,000); and rehabilitation of five staff houses (unit cost of about
US$6,000). The construction program will be financed by IDA, apart from Government's contribution
(estimated at US$0.07 million or 10% of total civil works cost), and local labor for the
construction/rehabilitation of staff housing, which would be provided by the communities under an agreement
between the communities and Government (estimated at US$0.07 million). Construction and rehabilitation
are expected to be completed in PY3. Because the contract values are small and the works are geographically
scattered, all civil works contracts, excluding the works related to the roofing and flooring replacement at the
USBs, of a total value of US$0.63 million equivalent including contingencies would be awarded on the basis
of competitive bidding advertised locally (LCB) in accordance with procedures acceptable to IDA. In
addition, civil works for some staff houses estimated to cost the equivalent of US$12,000 or less per contract
may be procured on the basis of price quotations obtained from at least three reliable suppliers provided that
the aggregate amount of such procurement does not exceed a total of US$0.06 million equivalent.
4.13          Goods. Goods financed under the project would include: vehicles, motorcycles, bicycles
(including repair kits), roofing and flooring materials, solar panels, furniture, office, audio-visual, and
medical equipment and supplies, and consumable goods such as transistor radios, T-shirts, and food packages
as part of the incentive scheme for community participation, for a total value of US$3.12 million equivalent
including contingencies. Most of the goods will be procured in PYI and PY2, and will be grouped into bid
packages of at least US$100,000 each and procured through international competitive bidding (ICB) in
accordance with the IDA's "Guidelines for Procurement under IBRD Loans and IDA Credits' (May 1992).
Contracts for goods procured through ICB would total about US$2.39 million, representing about 77% of the
total value of goods financed by IDA. Furniture which can be competitively procured locally and which
cannot be grouped into packages of at least US$100,000 equivalent each which, in the aggregate, would not
exceed US$0.42 million equivalent and would represent about 13% of the total value of goods financed by
IDA, would be suitable for procurement through LCB under procedures acceptable to IDA. The remaining
10% of the total value of goods, small equipment and materials, which cannot be grouped into bid packages
of at least US$50,000 equivalent will be procured on the basis of price quotations obtained from at least three
reliable suppliers, provided that the aggregate amount of such procurement does not exceed a total of
US$0.31 million equivalent.
4.14          Services. Service contracts for the construction of wells estimated to cost the equivalent of
US$6,000 or less per contract, may be procured under contracts awarded on the basis of comparison or price
quotations solicited from at least three suppliers, provided that the aggregate value of such contracts does not
exceed the equivalent of US$0.07 million.
4.15          Consultants financed by IDA, totalling US$2.48 million equivalent would be contracted in
accordance with IDA's "Guidelines for the Use of Consultants" (August 1981). The consultancy services
required under the project would be mostly for project management, coordination and implementation in the
PCU and SAFU, and for training activities. It would include, specifically, the services of locally recruited:
(a) training and management technical director for the coordination, management and implementation of the
training subproject; (b) architectural/procurement specialists for the design and supervision of the construction
program and for the preparation of bidding documents; and (c) accounting and auditing specialists for
establishing and monitoring the accounting system at the PCU and SAFU and for preparing the annual audit
report, respectively. Consultancy services to be financed by IDA would total about 488 person months. (A



- 29 -
list of specialist services included in the Social Sector Project and the Social Action Fund is found in
Annex 4-4). Terms of reference for major consulting services and details on consultancies are given in
Annexes 3-14 and 4-2.
4.16          The procurement methods to be followed for items financed under the Social Action Fund
cannot be defined in advance. The SAF Technical Committee will be responsible for reviewing and
approving proposals submitted by beneficiaries such as NGOs (paras. 3.23-3.24). The eligible proposals
along with the written agreements with the beneficiaries would be submitted to IDA, for its review and
approval, by the SAF Technical Committee. The negotiated agreements with the beneficiaries would include,
inter alia, the method of procurement to be used, which could be carried out under ICB, LCB, or prudent
shopping depending on the value of each procurement, and the method of payments to beneficiaries,
acceptable to IDA. IDA will undertake sample checking of such transactions. In addition a locally hired
assistant will visit sites where activities financed under the SAF are being carried out to ensure adequate use
of the funds. He will also monitor the consistency of the procurement procedures agreed. The Social Action
Fund will be financed entirely under the IDA credit. Items or groups of items not exceeding US$15,000; up
to an aggregate of US$0.4 million will be financed under the Social Action Fund.
4.17          Procurement Status of Ongoing Projects in Guinea-Bissau. IDA has made 21 credits to
Guinea-Bissau totalling US$188.1 million equivalent as of June 30, 1992, using SDR/US dollar rate at the
date of credit approval. Although Guinea-Bissau's institutional and technical capacity is limited, ongoing
projects in the country have not been severely hampered and disbursements on investment lending have taken
place as originally planned. However, three common implementation issues remain: (a) poor budget
management and lack of counterpart funds; (b) limited institutional capacity; and (c) limited aid coordination.
Actions to improve performance are being taken, including: (i) improving budgetary programming and
monitoring; (ii) establishment of a Public Investment Program executing unit; (iii) strengthening controls on
the ways counterpart funds are being generated which are mainly provided through the sale of food aid
received from donors; (iv) intensifying dialogue on procurement issues through specific missions and
seminars; (v) using the services of a procurement specialist and a consultant, on a case by case basis for
major contracts exceeding US$100,000; (vi) as part of terms-of-reference, requiring that all technical
assistants and project coordinators take specific actions on training and transfer of technology; (vii) increasing
use of mid-term review to address major problems and, if necessary, redesign project components; (viii)
strengthening training components; and (ix) increasing dialogue among the donor community and
Government. For the proposed project, a number actions are planned to minimize the problems that could
occur during implementation:
(a)    One PCU Project Director and one SAFU Managing Director have been locally recruited
under PPF financing and thereafter under the project to manage the Social Sector Project and
the Social Action Fund, respectively. Because of her experience in handling a Bank-financed
project, the current project manager for the ongoing Social and Infrastructure Relief Project
(SIRP, Cr. 2020-GUB) was offered the coordination of the preparation activities, on a part-
time basis, of the Social Sector Project until SIRP's closing date (June 30, 1993). At that
time she will assume project director activities for the Social Sector Project on a full-time
basis. Past experience in project management and procurement was taken into consideration
by Government for the selection of this candidate. Similarly, for the recruitment of the
SAFU Managing Director, special attention was given to the experience of the candidate
selected in dealing or working with NGOs;
(b)    The PCU Project Director has the responsibility for heading the PCU and the routine
administration, management and day-to-day coordination of unit for the Social Sector Project.
In addition, the PCU Project Director will work closely with the Project Implementation



- 30 -
Committee in coordinating the various aspects of the project's implementation and would, in
particular, handle all administrative and contractual procedures necessary for the
implementation of project activities. She will be assisted by a locally recruited Training and
Management Technical Director, Procurement/ Architectural Specialist, and project
accountant who will have the direct responsibility for coordinating and implementing the
activities related to the Training and IEC subprojects, the Rehabilitation/Equipping
subproject, and establishing and monitoring the accounting system for the PCU, respectively.
In addition, a Project Implementation Committee consisting of the PCU Project Director, the
Training and Management Technical Director, the Procurement/Architectural Specialist, and
MINPH Director of Planning, and the Director of Primary Health Care has been created in
order to provide policy guidance and facilitate implementation;
(c)    The SAFU Managing Director will manage and control the funds of the SAF as well as
administer the daily activities of the unit. He is to be assisted by a locally hired assistant who
will also assist beneficiaries and national NGOs in preparing project proposals as well as
visiting sites where activities financed under the SAF are being carried out to supervise and
monitor project activities. A full-time accountant will be locally hired to establish and
monitor finances for the SAF.
(d)    In order to minimize delays in project implementation, a detailed implementation schedule for
works, goods and services to be procured under the project was prepared at the time of
appraisal (Annex 4-5; During implementation, all bid evaluation reports transmitted to IDA
for review will contain an updated copy of the implementation schedule; and
(e)    A procurement/disbursement workshop organized by IDA, with the assistance of the PCU,
will take place upon credit effectiveness for all project staff concerned.
4.18          Procurement Arrangements Under the Proposed Project. The PCU Project Director will be
responsible for procurement for the Social Sector Project, including the SAFU. She will be periodically
supported by an internationally-recruited procurement/architectural specialist, as necessary (i.e., two person
months before credit effectiveness, and three person months per year during the first three years of the
project). At negotiations, the Project Director and the locally recruited procurement/architectural specialist
submitted to IDA in draft form the bidding documents for civil works.
4.19          The PCU Project Director assisted by the procurement/architectural specialists (foreign and
local) will develop a simple monitoring system to help collect and record procurement information as follows:
(a) prompt reporting of contract award information based on the agreed implementation schedule; and (b)
comprehensive semi-annual reports to IDA, indicating: (i) revised cost estimates for individual contracts and
the total project, including best estimates of allowances for physical and price contingencies; (ii) revised
timing of procurement actions, including advertising, bidding, contract award, and completion time for
individual contracts; and (iii) compliance with aggregate limits on specified methods of procurement.
4.20          Bank Review. Prior review by IDA would be required for procurement of packages of
works, goods and services which exceed US$100,000. The review process would cover about 80% of the
total value of the amount contracted for works, goods and services financed by IDA. Selective post-review of
awarded contracts below the threshold levels would apply to about one in three works contracts, and one in
six goods and services contracts. In all cases of ICB procurement, the draft bidding documents and proposed
contract awards would also require IDA's prior review. LCB procedures will include: (a) explicit statement
to bidders of the evaluation and award criteria; (b) local advertising with public bid opening; (c) no
preference to state-owned enterprises; (d) award to lowest bidder; and (e) foreign bidders would not be



- 31 -
precluded from participating in LCB. IDA's prior concurrence would be sought with regards to the terms-of-
reference, qualifications and estimated cost of consultants to be recruited under the project.
4.21             During negotiations, agreement was reached on the proper monitoring system for
procurement (para. 4.19). The Government gave assurances that it will apply the procurement procedures
and arrangements outlined above.
H.        Disbursements
4.22             The proposed credit is expected to be completed over a four-year period, and the IDA credit
would be disbursed over a four and one-half year period, compared to Guinea-Bissau's standard disbursement
profile of six and one-half years. The fact that an operational PCU with an experienced project director is
already in place and is administering project activities under a PPF, should further facilitate the project's
start-up activities upon credit effectiveness. The estimated schedule of disbursements is shown in Annex 46,
and is compared to the standard country disbursement profile for all sectors in Guinea-Bissau.
4.23             The table below gives the categories and amounts to be financed out of the IDA credit, and
the percentage to be financed in each category.

4.24             All withdrawal applications will be fully documented except for: (a) payments under
contracts of less than $20,000; (b) local training-related costs; and (c) incremental recurrent costs,
which will be done on the basis of Statement of Expenditures (SOEs). Documentation for
withdrawals under SOEs would be retained at the PCU for review by IDA staff during supervision
missions and for regular audits.

- 32 -
4.25          Special Account. To facilitate disbursements of eligible expenditures for goods and
services, the Government would open a Special Account at a commercial bank to cover IDA's share
of eligible expenditures to be managed and administered exclusively by the PCU. The authorized
allocation for the Special Account would be US$500,000; IDA would make an initial deposit for that
amount from the proposed credit immediately upon credit effectiveness. IDA would replenish the
Special Account on a monthly basis upon receipt of satisfactory proof of incurred eligible
expenditures. Replenishment requests would be accompanied by up-to-date bank statements and
reconciliations of the Special Account. All payments under $50,000 equivalent must be made through
the Special Account; payments above that amount may be made by the direct payment or special
commitment procedure.
4.26          Proiect Account. The establishment of a Project Account in pesos, in a local
commercial bank, for counterpart funds and an initial deposit of US$60,000 equivalent to cover
sufficiently Government's share for initiating project activities would be a condition of effectiveness.
This account would be administered by the PCU Project Director. The Government would be
required to make semi-annually deposits of local counterpart funds in advance.
4.27          Disbursement Monitoring. Locally hired accountants will be appointed to establish
and monitor an accounting system for the Social Sector Project, including the Social Action Fund.
They will be assisted by a foreign accountant as necessary. During project implementation, the PCU
project accountant, assisted by the SAFU accountant, will have the responsibility for consolidating the
Social Sector Project and Social Action Fund transactions for the purpose of the annual audit as well
as preparing yearly budgetary projections for the duration of the project.
I.    Accounting. Auditing, and Reporting
4.28          Overall responsibility for the Social Sector Project's, including the SAF, financial
management would rest with the PCU, which would maintain the consolidated project accounts for
the entire project and detailed accounts for each subproject. Under the overall responsibility of the
PCU Director, the PCU would prepare and submit to IDA, covering the entire project, inclusive of
each subproject, and the SAF with input provided by the SAFU Director: (a) quarterly progress
reports on project implementation; (b) annual financial statements on project expenditures; (c) annual
work programs and budgets for the following year; and (d) a project completion report within six
months of the credit closing date. Project accounts, including the Special Account and disbursements
under SOEs, would be audited annually by independent auditors acceptable to IDA. This includes a
separate annual audit of the SAF, also carried out by independent auditors acceptable to IDA. The
audit reports would be submitted to IDA within six months of the end of each fiscal year. During
negotiations, the Government gave assurances that it would submit to IDA an annual audit report, of
reasonable scope and detail, within six months of the end of the Government's fiscal year.
4.29          No later than six months after the closing date of the project the Borrower will
prepare a Project Completion Report (PCR) in a form satisfactory to IDA.



- 33 -
V. PROJECT BENEFITS AND RISKS
5.1            The main benefit of the project would be to improve the delivery and quality of
primary health care for a greater proportion of the population, particularly the most vulnerable groups
at the periphery, and, thereby, have a positive effect on the social indicators. Direct benefits of the
proposed project will be the physical and human capital improvements resulting from the subprojects,
and strengthening the capacity of some institutions charged with delivering social services. In health
the project will include comprehensive training programs for health workers, physically improving
and equipping selected health facilities, creating better links between health centers and village health
posts, and improving the coordination and delivery of health information, education and
communications messages to the general population for critical topics such as AIDS, nutrition, and
family planning. Through the SAF the project would improve coordination among the various entities
working in the social sectors, including social sector ministries, donors and NGOs, and community
groups, while promoting greater regional and local participation. The SAF is designed to reach those
at the grassroots level and the poor by providing support for micro-social sector projects including
income generating activities for women, health and nutrition, maternal and child health programs,
family planning activities, and literacy and skills development, among others.
5.2           The proposed operation has two significant risks. The first risk involves the limited
managerial and implementation capabilities of the Government and the primary institutions involved.
This risk is being minimized by keeping the subprojects simple in design. It is also being minimized
by establishing a Project Implementation Committee, under the Ministry of Public Health, with
technical representatives and coordinators responsible for implementing the health sector subprojects,
and a SAF Technical Committee reporting to the SAFU Management/Technical Director who will
implement the SAF subproject. A strong and experienced Project Coordinating Unit, located within
the Ministry of Foreign Affairs and Cooperation will assist both ministries in the implementation of
project activities. The PCU will be strengthened and derive a number of benefits from being
converted from an existing project management unit for an IDA project that will be completed in June
1993. The new project will take advantage of the experience of some existing staff, including a
national coordinator who is now familiar with IDA procedures. Further, the PCU, which now has
the Project Director in place and is operational with PPF funds, will not experience delays in start-up
activities once the project becomes effective. In addition, technical assistance and in-service training
will be provided to PCU staff and key implementing departments throughout the project period.
Further, an award system will be developed for PCU staff. This will consist of a competitive yearly
merit review of PCU staff against measurable performance criteria based on an implementation
schedule and measurable indicators for key project activities. The second risk is the lack of sufficient
counterpart funds and the overall financial sustainability of the project. These risks will be addressed
by setting targets for increases in central budget allocations to the social sectors and monitoring social
spending as part of the project conditionality; and, to ensure sufficient counterpart funds local labor
and materials, through the community participation element built into the project, will count towards
part of the counterpart contribution, as will grant money from donors for the SAF.



- 34 -
VI. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATION
6.1           Prior to negotiations, the Government: (a) submitted a draft health policy
implementation plan, including an operational plan, related costs, and timetable for implementing the
strategy; a health financing plan integrating cost recovery measures and the planned expansion of
these measures, as well as a time-bound action program to open-up and regulate private health
practice; including a budget increase for the Ministry of Public Health for each year (1993-97); (b)
submitted a detailed framework to implement the Social Action Fund, with the main features of the
Fund agreed during negotiations (paras. 3.21-3.22); (c) submitted draft standard bidding documents
for construction/rehabilitation (para. 4.18); (d) held the first meeting of the Social Sector Project's
Project Implementation Committee (para. 4.2); (e) made proposals for specific incentives and a plan
and timetable to implement such proposals, within the context of the Government's wage
policy/compensation system, to correct the urban/rural imbalance and attract health care workers to
move from Bissau to the regions (paras. 2.24-2.25, 3-15).
6.2           As agreed during negotiations, the Government has: (a) established a project
coordination unit staffed with experienced and qualified personnel (paras. 4.2-4.3, and 4.17 (a)-(b));
(b) established a social action fund unit staffed with experienced and qualified persornel (para. 4.5);
(c) issued an official resolution creating the SAF and adopted an operational framework for
administering the Fund; and (d) issued a health policy implementation plan (para. 2.32).
6.3           During negotiations, the Government gave assurances that it will:
(a)    deposit counterpart funds semi-annually in advance in the project account (para.
4.26);
(b)    allocate and make available in a timely manner the national budgetary resources for
the health sector annually as agreed with IDA (para. 3.27);
(c)   submit by October 31 of each year, the proposed budget and its composition for the
health sector for the next year for review with IDA, and that the allocations will be
made in agreed amounts (para. 3.27);
(d)   review the training programs carried out each year under the project with IDA, and
submit to IDA for review by the Ministry of Public Health, in collaboration with the
Project's Training and Management Technical Director, by October 31 of each year,
the proposed training programs to be implemented under the project for the next year
(paras. 4.8-4.9);
(e)   submit quarterly progress reports by the PCU to IDA for review and comments (para.
4.8);
(f)   submit annual audits for the project of reasonable scope and detail, within six months
of the end of the Government's fiscal year, which include separate opinions of the
auditors on the Special Account and SOEs; and, annual audits of the SAF (para.
4.28);
(g)    assign exclusive responsibility for management and administration of the Special
Account with the PCU (para. 4.25);



- 35 -
(h)    carry out a mid-term review by July 1995, which focuses on implementation of the
health sector policy implementation plan including an analysis of key monitorable
indicators, progress of the training programs, rehabilitation of health facilities, and
status of progress of private health practice (para. 4.9);
(i)    maintain the Social Sector Project's Project Implementation Committee during the
project which would meet on a regular basis (para. 4.2);
(j)    organize, in coordination with IDA, every year of project implementation a donors'
meeting to review progress in social programs and implementation of the plan
according to the agreed schedule (paras. 3.1 and 4.6); and
(k)    use procurement procedures and arrangements as outlined in the project documents
and implement a proper monitoring system for procurement (para. 4.21).
Conditions for Credit Effectiveness
6.4           The following would be conditions for Credit Effectiveness:
(a)   the Government would have established a Project Account in Pesos in a local
commercial bank for counterpart funds and make an initial deposit of US$60,000
equivalent (4.26);
(b)   the Government would have appointed a project accountant and a
procurement/architectural specialist satisfactory to IDA (paras. 4.3 and 4.17 (b));
(c)   the Ministry of Public Health would have adopted a health sector budget for 1993
with a composition of expenditures satisfactory to IDA (para. 3.27); and
(d)   the Government would have sought expert assistance to develop indicators to assess
the effectiveness and formulate a detailed plan to regularly coordinate, supervise and
monitor the progress of the health training programs and rehabilitation of health
facilities under the project, satisfactory to IDA (paras. 4.4 and 4.8).
6.5           Subject to the agreements to the above, the proposed project is suitable for an IDA
credit of SDR 6.4 million (US$8.8 million equivalent) to the Republic of Guinea-Bissau on standard
IDA terms with a maturity of 40 years.
Population and Human Resources Division
Western Africa Department
Africa Region
