REPUBLIC OF GUINEA-BISSAU
SOCIAL SECTOR PROJECT
Health Policy Implementation Plan
Statement on the National Health Policy
I.    Introduction
The health situation is subordinated to the economic and financial crisis the country is undergoing
and to the structural adjustment that the Government is implementing jointly with multilateral and bilateral
cooperation agencies in order to stabilize the economy.
The predominant public health problems continue to be malaria, acute diarrheal diseases, acute
respiratory infections, tuberculosis, leprosy, onchocerciasis, bilharziasis, measles, malnutrition, and
tetanus neonatorum. The AIDS pandemic has also become worrisome among sexually transmitted
diseases. The main indicators that characterize the country's health situation are:
- Infant mortality: 140 per 1,000 live births;
- Maternal mortality: 914 per 100,000 live births;
- Vaccine coverage of children 12 to 23 months:
3 BCG 94%
3 DPT3 54%
e Polio 54%
3 Measles 65%
* 1T2 38.8%
- Life expectancy at birth: 47.1 years
- Coverage of drinking water supply: 22.5% of population
- Sanitation: 30% in urban areas
18% in rural areas.
Agriculture is an essential component of the national economy, accounting for approximately
three-fourths of export earnings and employing approximately 80% of the country's economically active
population. The other principal sectors of production are fisheries and silviculture. The social sectors
are largely dependent on external cooperation. For example the health sector is financed approximately
90% by multi-bilateral cooperation. The share of the general state budget earmarked to the health sector
is approximately 8% in 1992, despite the priority attributed to this important sector.
The guiding principles of national health policy are based on primary health care, with the
objective of health for all the people of Guinea-Bissau. The essential elements of the policy of health
development from now to the year 2000 are: strengthening the national health system at the central,
regional, and local levels; decentralizing the national health service; strengthening the capacity for
management of health services, programs, and units; judicious census and redistribution of personnel;
ongoing activities for technical and professional training and improvement; rehabilitating and equipping
the health infrastructures; the supply, management, and rational use of essential drugs; community
participation; and mobilization of internal and external resources.



- 43 -
ANNEX 2-4
Page 3 of 36
II.    Health Sector Policy
The policy of the health sector is an integral part of Government policy for overall socioeconomic
development of the country. In this context the objective is to implement the essential components of
primary health care nationwide. The principal strategy is based on strengthening the national health
system, with sound articulation among the central, regional, and local levels, with functioning structures
and institutions, using appropriate scientific and technological knowledge, and responding to the needs
of the population for preventive and curative health care. In this area intersectoral collaboration is key.
The capacity of the sector to collect, analyze, and use health information will have to be
strengthened in order to create an adequate base for the management of health programs and services.
Strengthening the management capacity at the central, regional, and local levels will make it possible to
improve the preparation, implementation, monitoring, and evaluation of the national health policy, the
strategic plan for health development, the annual plans of action, and the operational plans of the
country's health regions, with a view to attaining the social objective of "health for all."
Research in the health sector should come to play a more effective role in the development of the
national health system, and especially at the operational level it will seek to identify the approaches,
strategies, and technologies appropriate for the geographic, economic, and sociocultural context of
Guinea-Bissau. Valuation of the positive aspects of traditional medicine and pharmacopoeia of Guinea-
Bissau and also taking into account the context of the national health policy.
In the context of reviewing its strategic plan for health development in 1991 the Government set
forth the following operational objectives, to be attained by 1996:
a) to decentralize health services, strengthen health institutions, and restore and equip the health
structures regionally and locally, guaranteeing preferential allocation of resources for these levels;
b) to introduce the process of management for health development in all the Regions and
strengthen the operation of the health system in 90% of the country's administrative sectors;
c) to develop a program for the training and recycling of personnel, and correct the distortions
in the distribution of personnel, taking into account the specific needs and general objectives of
the sector;
d) to establish and implement an incentives systems that facilitates keeping staff in rural areas,
with special emphasis on the areas relatively more isolated from the capital city;
e) to strengthen coordination of the different elements of the national health system at the central
level and integrate the primary health care activities at the local level;
f) to develop a national system of health information, including epidemiological surveillance;
g) to place absolute priority on the Maternal and Child Health programs, including Family
Planning, Drinking Water Supply and Environmental Sanitation, and Disease Prevention and
Control; promote mechanisms of intersectoral collaboration for health development;
h) to develop the private sector of medicine without detriment to the public sector;
i) to prepare an up-to-date study on the financing of health services; determine the costs of the
medical services provided in hospital institutions, define and apply a strategy for cost recovery;
expand the Bamako Initiative in the framework of the national drug policy to all Regions of the
country;



- 44 -
ANNEX 2-4
P'age 4 of 36
j) to strengthen coordination among the different cooperation agencies in the area of health with
a view to optimizing technical and financial assistance; progressively increase the State budget
for the health sector;
k) to pass health legislation to support implementation of the health policy;
1) to pay special attention to mental health, the health of schoolage children, and oral-dental health
in the context of primary health care;
m) to develop, together with related agencies, a system for warning and intervention in disaster
situations, ensuring the material and human resources needed for emergency medicine in disaster
situations;
n) to reorient and improve coordination of the scarce resources available for research in public
health; the various dispersed initiatives should be concentrated in a single national institution for
public health research;
o) to establish the mechanisms for coordinating the activities of information, education, and
communication among the health programs, and promote intersectoral collaboration in this area.
p) to develop a policy for the rehabilitation and maintenance of infrastructure and equipment, for
which it will be necessary to establish a technical capacity at the central and regional levels,
making possible intervention in all health units nationwide. This plan will no doubt make it
possible to improve the delivery of health care.
III.   Priority Programs
MATERNAL AND CHILD HEALTH/FAMILY PLANNING (MCH/FP)
This program has the objective of promoting the progressive improvement of maternal and child
health, considering mother and child as individuals, family members, and members of the community,
thanks to the implementation of integrated MCH/FP activities at every level. Concretely, through this
program an effort is underway to reduce infant mortality by 30% and to reduce the current rate of
maternal mortality by 50% by the year 2000. The principal strategies of this Program will be:
a) coordination at the central level, among the various parties intervening in management and
financing;
b) preparation of operational plans for each Region integrated into the primary health care
activities and with active community involvement;
c) formulation, revision, and dissemination of technical standards and management of the
program, through seminars, meetings, or joint supervisory visits of MCH personnel at the
maternal and child health centers, maternity units, and health centers;
d) supervision and evaluation of the operational plans for the health sectors, including the
selection, recycling, supervision, and support for the traditional birth attendants.
The initiatives for risk-free maternity and child survival activities will be encouraged in the
communities where the health system has the minimum elements needed for its functioning. This major
program is a priority. Its main components will be the following:
* Prenatal and delivery health care
* Expanded Program on Immunization



- 45 -                   ~~~aNNEX 2-t
i'age 5 of f6
 Promotion of Food and Nutrition
* Prevention and control of Acute Diarrheal Diseases
* Prevention and control of Acute Respiratory Infections
* Family Planning
* Health Information and Education in the Schools
* Monitoring Growth from 0 to 12 months.
Thus the objectives to be attained by the year 2000 are:
* To reduce infant mortality from 140 per 1,000 live births to 98 per 1,000;
* To reduce maternal mortality from 914 per 100,000 live births to 457 per 100,000;
* To promote spacing between births of at least two years, reaching 70% of new mothers;
* To increase by 70% prenatal care coverage to term;
* To promote institutional delivery or delivery attended by health personnel with technical
training, increasing coverage from 10% to 50%;
* To reduce the prevalence of moderate protein-calorie malnutrition from 40% to 20% and to
eliminate the current prevalence (2.4%) of severe malnutrition;
* To reduce by 50% the rate of mortality due to diarrheal diseases in children under 5 years of
age;
* To provide oral rehydration salts to 80% of children under 5 years of age with diarrheal
diseases;
* To analyze the risk factors of Acute Respiratory Infections and to develop as a result a strategy
to control them with a view to reducing by 50% current mortality due to acute respiratory
infections;
* To increase vaccine coverage (EPI) from 60% to 90%; and for women of reproductive age,
from 43.8% to 70%;
* To eradicate poliomyelitis and to eliminate tetanus neonatorum;
* To promote information, education, and communication (IEC) activities based on the technical
guidelines of WHO and taking into account the country's socio-cultural context.
2.    THE SUPPLY OF POTABLE WATER, AND ENVIRONMENTAL SANITATION
Diseases associated with deficiencies in the supply of potable water and environmental sanitation
services are among the principal reasons why people consult clinics, dispensaries, and hospitals in this
country. These waterborne diseases and others that result from unhealthful environmental conditions not
only constitute a health problem; they have an effect on the economy because of the lengthy periods of
worker disability that they often cause. In this context, the supply of potable water and environmental
sanitation services is considered an integral component of preventive medicine. The Ministry of Public
Health bases its principal strategy for increasing the coverage of these services and improving the health



- 46 -
Page 6 of 36
of the general public on intersectorial cooperation with the Ministry of Public Works, the Ministry of
Natural Resources, the Ministry of Rural Development and Agriculture, the State Tourism Secretariat,
the City Council of Bissau, and Regional Government Committees.
Available data indicate that the rate of coverage of water supply services in urban areas is 48
percent, but only 30 percent for sanitation services. In rural areas, we have 18 percent coverage for
water supply and 19 percent for sanitation. Considering that the latest assessment of services coverage
was done more than ten years ago, the Ministry of Public Health is giving priority to a new national
appraisal to update these indicators.
Considering the results of the appraisal, the Ministry of Public Health has set the following as
its priorities and objectives for the year 2000:
(a)    Enhancing the Department of Hygiene and Sanitation by strengthening its
institutional capacity and its spheres of authority through personnel training and
technical/materials supply activities; also, ensuring a decentralization of this
department;
(b)   Increasing sanitation services in rural areas and on the outskirts of Bissau by 30
percent, and in urban areas by 40%;
(c)    Increasing water supply in the urban and rural environments by 100 percent;
(d)    Making quality controls on water intended for human consumption operational
in urban and rural zones, aiming at a minimum 30 percent coverage;
(e)    Supplying 50 percent of health centers with potable water by using solar energy;
(f)    Intensifying public information and education activities on the subjects of water
use and consumption, sanitation services, and environmental protection.
3.    DISEASE PREVENTION AND CONTROL
13.1  Malaria Prevention and Control
Malaria is the main cause of general mortality and morbidity in this country. It is one of the
principal factors in the high rates of infant and maternal mortality. The bulk of the absences from work
and school are likewise attributable to this disease. Available epidemiological data are not reliable, or
even indicative, of the magnitude of the problem. However, a general idea can be gleaned from the
figures supplied by the Pediatrics Department of the Simao Mendes National Hospital. In 1991, 3,060
children were hospitalized there with malaria; 300 of them died, which reflects a high death rate, about
10 percent.
Malaria is also blamed for low birth weights, for which the average estimate for both sexes is
12.4 percent. With technical assistance from the WHO, the Government has developed an Anti-Malaria
Plan of Action with a time horizon through 1997, budgeted at US$3.5 million. This plan of action, now
in the process of obtaining Government approval, will later be submitted to the donors.
Objectives
*     Increase to 100 percent coverage and improve public access to early and correct
diagnosis and treatment, in the context of primary health care;
*     Reduce the incidence of serious and complicated cases of malaria, as well as the
high rates of mortality caused by the disease, especially in the under-five age
group and in pregnant women;
*     Train or 'recycle" the technical personnel working at health centers, teaching
them methods of clinical and laboratory diagnosis, epidemiological treatment and
monitoring, including drug-resistant cases.



- 47 -
ANNEX 2-4
3.2    Campaign Against Tuberculosis and Leprosy                 Page 7 of 36
Tuberculosis is one of the main causes of morbidity in this country.  The number of reported
cases rose from 752 in 1987 to 1,246 in 1991. Since 1980, the country has had a Program to Control
Tuberculosis and Leprosy (TUB/LEP), which operates under the supervision and with the support of the
central government. The general objectives of this program are:
(a)    Reducing human suffering and preventing death and disability caused by
tuberculosis;
(b)    Curbing the transmission of tuberculosis and leprosy, and significantly reducing
the social problems they cause;
(c)    Increase ability to track, treat, and monitor cases at 70 percent of the health
centers;
(d)    Reduce the rate of abandonment of treatment to less than 25 percent;
(e)    Expand coverage of the health center services to 70 percent.
Infection with the HIV virus is a risk factor.  A study on the prevalence of HIV among
tuberculosis patients, done by the National TUB/LEP Program, showed that about 20 percent of these
patients are seropositive.
3.3    Program to Control Onchocerciasis
This is an endemic disease that is restricted to the eastern regions of this country, and most
prevalent in the region of Gabd. The country has a Program to Control Onchocerciasis, with assistance
from WHO/OCP. However it is in the process of being returned to national authorities since the
prevalence of the disease is less than 5 percent, which satisfies the return criteria established by the OCP
and the JPC. A plan for the return of this program was drafted and submitted to the donors. Under this
program, the program to control onchocerciasis is being integrated into primary health care and linked
to the program to control malaria and schistosomiasis. Provision is also made for components pertaining
to management, training, and reinforcement/equipment of the sanitation infrastructures in the regions of
Gabd and BafatA. This is an integrated program of great interest to the socioeconomic development of
the eastern regions of this country.
13.4   Campaign Against AIDS and other Sexually-Transmitted Diseases
The STD are, in general, endemic to this country. HIV and AIDS infections are a source of
concern, given the upward trend that began with the identification of the first seropositive cases and of
the disease itself. The predominant virus is HIV-2, but rare cases of HIV-1, and even mixed infections,
have been observed. Heterosexual contact is the most common means of transmission; the male/female
ratio is 1:1. In 1989, rates of serum prevalence found during office visits by pregnant women and among
blood donors ranged between 5 and 9 percent in the Autonomous Sector of Bissau. A National Campaign
Against AIDS is being carried out with technical and financial assistance from WHO, ASDI, and other
donors. The chief components of the program are: management, resource mobilization, information,
education and use of the media, epidemiological monitoring, quality assurance for transfused blood,
clinical support, nursing advice and care. The Ministry of Public Health will develop a single common
strategy for the campaign against STD and AIDS.
Objectives
*      Inform, increase awareness, and educate the public as to the existence, means of
transmission, and ways to prevent infection and illness;
*      Prevention of transmission by transfusion;
*      Counseling for persons who have undergone HIV testing;



- 48 -                          ANNEX  2-4
*     Counseling for family members of AIDS patients;    Page 8 of 36
*     Integration of the battle against STD and AIDS into the realm of primary health
care down to the local level (the individual, the family, and the community);
*     Improvements in knowledge as to the epidemiological status of the disease.
3.5   INFORMATION, EDUCATION, AND COMMUNICATION (IEC)
This country benefits from a variety of information, education, and media activities. Prominent
among them is the strengthening of coordination among the parties involved in this area, and the
consequent development of the productivity of the available equipment and human resources. To that
end, an inter-sectorial structure or mechanism for coordinating IEC activities needs to be created. With
such an initiative, educational messages will reach the public at more regular intervals and contribute
more effectively to the transmission of knowledge, and to promoting attitudes and practices more
beneficial to health. All health programs should have an IEC component.
3.6   POLICIES ON MATTERS OF ESSENTIAL DRUGS. In 1976, the Ministry of Public Health
drafted a National Health Plan in which objectives and strategies in the pharmaceutical field were defined.
The intent was to make high-quality drugs available nationwide and accessible to the entire population.
In 1978, pharmaceutical policy was oriented toward the Essential Medicines Program, which was financed
mainly by UNICEF, ASDI, and also by the WHO. To implement this strategy and improve the
organization of the purchasing process, government importation of medicines was nationalized and
entrusted to one company-FARMEDI--which was awarded exclusive rights to import for the private and
public sectors. In 1987, the Government opted for a policy of economic liberalization, which helped spur
the opening of new private pharmacies; however legislation to regulate both the licensing process and the
procedures for importing drugs and similar products had to be enacted and published. In 1989, the
pharmaceutical legislation was approved by the Council of Ministers. The Ministry of Public Health,
with the support of foreign assistance and with contributions equivalent to US$70,000 (5 percent of the
total spending on medicines) from the Government Budget, has endeavored to assure the population--even
those on its most distant fringes--access to essential medicines. These are distributed judiciously
according to the needs and areas of responsibility of the different levels of the national health service.
The extension of the Bamako Initiative to the entire country (starting in 1993), the recovery of costs of
hospital services, and the ABOTA system will permit improving the system for maintaining the supply
of medicines and the quality of the medical services rendered. Other factors--such as improvement of
management and controls regarding drugs, training and advanced skills mastery by personnel in this field-
-will contribute decisively to the most rational use of the drugs, and consequently benefit the pursuit of
'health for everyone."
IV.   Organization of the National Health System based on Primary Health Care
The Ministry of Public Health is the State agency that sets the direction for national health policy,
to which all elements of the health system should consider as the point of reference for developing their
activities nationwide. The national health system is structured on three levels: Central (national),
Regional, and Local (administrative sector).
IV. 1 - CENTRAL LEVEL
The main role of this level is to formulate policies and strategies and to translate them into master
plans and programs to guide the health actions of the Regions. The Ministry of Public Health is currently
being strengthened. This effort is reflected in:
a) organization of an administrative and technical-scientific staff to mobilize and use resources
for primary, secondary, and tertiary health care by order of priority;



- 49 -
ANNEX 2-4
Page 9 of 36
b) coordination of the activities and services in charge of managing the health programs, stacction
of appropriate health technologies, and establishment of standards;
c) improvement of management of the entire system through the departments in charge of
planning, evaluation, supervision, information, documentation, and operations research;
d) intersectoral coordination for interministerial and multidisciplinary interventions linked to
health development;
e) approval and publication of the Organic Law of the Ministry of Public Health.
IV.2 - REGIONAL LEVEL
This level generally coincides with the country's political-administrative Regions, and has as its
principal function transforming the strategic plans, national programs, and master plans of the central
level into operational plans and activities. The regional structures in charge of implementing the health
system are the Committees of State of the Regions (representatives of the Central Government in the
Regions), the Regional Health Offices (and the Regional Health Commissions to be created). The
Regional Health Offices will act in three fundamental areas: hospital care, public health, and intersectoral
collaboration for the health of populations.
IV.2.1. Regional HosDjtal
In the regions hospital care will be provided in regional hospitals which may be public or
religious, with a management team including at least one physician-director, one chief nurse, and one
administrator. The regional hospital will provide at least the following services: outpatient consultations
by generalists, stomatology, and nursing care; a pharmacy, a laboratory, a radiology unit, and other
services; and care for hospitalized patients in the areas of general medicine, pediatrics, surgery, and
obstetrics.
IV.2.2. Regional Center for Primary Health Care
The principal function of these regional structures is to provide technical support for the
implementation of primary health care in the Health Centers. The articulation between the network of
health centers and the regional hospital and vice versa, and the horizontal linkage with other health-related
sectors (education, agriculture, natural resources, public works, and urban development) shall also be
done at this level. Here the Regional Health Teams operate under the leadership of the Regional Health
Directors, who represent the Ministry of Public Health in the Regions.
IV.3 - LOCAL LEVEL
This level coincides with the political-administrative sectors of the country, which have the
following characteristics:
a) they are clearly-defined administrative zones made up of a population and a local government
that acts by delegation of the Regional State Committee;
b) it is the level closest to the community;
c) it is the operational level of primary health care, in the health centers.
The structures for implementation of the health system at this level are the Sector State Committee
(highest political-administrative authority), the Sector Health Team, and the Sector Health Commission
to be created. The principal attributes at this level are the development and management of the health
system, preparation of the commnunity health plans, organization of the community health activities, and



- 50 -
ANNEX  2-4
Page 10 of 36
support for and monitoring of health activities at the community, family, and individual levels. The
community structures of reference at this level are the Grass-roots Health Units, built and operating at
the initiative of the communities, with support from the State.
IV.4 OTHER ELEMENTS OF THE NATIONAL HEALTH SYSTEM
This chapter will consider the private network of medical offices, nursing posts, pharmacies,
dental offices, Religious and Military Hospitals and Health Centers, health NGOs, institutions offering
medical and technical-medical education, national and foreign research institutions including single entities
or sub-systems of traditional medicine/pharmacopoeia, and the organs or agencies for multisectoral
cooperation for health development.
V.  HEALTH HUMAN RESOURCES DEVELOPMENT
At present, and in general, there is an unequal distribution of human resources, with a tendency
to concentrate in the capital city, there is also a second salient feature, which has to do with the need for
constant technical improvement of the existing technical personnel. At present there is one physician per
8,000 population. There are 250 general nurses, 714 auxiliary nurses, 50 midwives, 85 auxiliary
midwives, 149 laboratory technicians (including auxiliaries). The Government in this special chapter is
formulating a strategy for the development of human resources in health, which will include in detail the
following aspects:
a) updated repertory of personnel, by categories and locales where placed;
b) definition of the staff, based on the needs of the health development process;
c) establishing goals for medical and technical-medical training in function of needs;
d) instituting permanent training for technical and administrative personnel to facilitate technical
and professional improvement and implementation of a policy of promoting, stimulating, and
delegating responsibilities to national staff;
e) correction as soon as possible of the distortions between the central and regional levels and
among the regions themselves, with respect to the distribution of staff;
f) short-term training of physicians with specialties in general surgery, internal medicine,
gynecology and obstetrics, pediatrics, and public health, placing priority on in-country training,
where the conditions should be created for this purpose; proceed in the same direction in relation
to specialized training in nursing and other technical-medical specialties;
g) development of the combination of care-teaching-research in the medical and paramedical
programs of study;
h) creation of a directing staff, staff of the administrative structures, and staff for implementing
national health policy, developing their competences, and provide the material basis for carrying
out their functions.
V. 1   Incentives Policy
One of the difficulties in retaining Ministry of Public Health personnel in the regions is the
remoteness of and difficult access to some zones of the interior.
To retain personnel, incentives must be created so as to make it possible not only for health staff
to remain in those locales, but also to induce spontaneous requests for transfer to the interior. This will
make it possible to reduce the over-concentration of health personnel in the Autonomous Sector of Bissau.
The incentive could take various forms, such as a bonus equal to a certain percentage of the
salary (subsidy for working in remote areas) and provision of adequate housing.



- 51 -                           ANNEX  2-4
Page 11 of 36
To provide such housing, the Ministry of Public Health has been building health units with
housing for the head nurse and midwife. At this time there are 50 health centers with lodging facilities,
of which 13 are new and distributed in three regions of the country.
To come up with the subsidy for working in remote areas, a three-tier system was designed based
on degree of remoteness, to be given a bonus of 200,000, 100,000, and 50,000 Guinea-Bissau pesos
respectively (see annex). Beginning in March of this year this policy will be implemented on a small
scale, i.e. 50,000 and 35,000 respectively for the most remote and intermediate zones, which will make
it possible to progressively keep nurses, midwives, and other mid-level and auxiliary personnel in the
rural areas, while awaiting approval of greater amounts to proceed to implementation of this plan as of
July 1993.
VI. HEALTH CARE FINANCING SYSTEM
14.    Health services are financed almost entirely by funds from the Government Budget, of
which the health sector has been given an average of 8 percent in the past five years, and from
international cooperation (multilateral assistance - 58.7 percent; bilateral assistance - 19.2 percent; and
foreign NGO's, 22.1 percent), representing about 90 percent of the total financing of the sector. Per
capita virtual spending on health is about US$12.00/per annum.
The scarcity of funds available for health from the Government Budget obviously has disastrous
effects on the categories of expenses that must be defrayed by the State, particularly employee
compensation, minor maintenance expenses, travel, and other outlays essential to the normal functioning
of a country's health administration structure. As a result, we experience low productivity among poorly-
motivated personnel, and we witness a constant deterioration in the condition of the sanitation
infrastructure, equipment, vehicles, etc.
The Structural Adjustment Program now underway in this country demands financial austerity,
and that has had a negative impact on the budget for the social sector. The health sector has been heavily
penalized (note the 27.4 percent decline in the government budget for health, from US$3,748,300 in 1986
to US$1,737,800 in 1990). This has further exacerbated the existing imbalance between the categories
of expenditures that are financed by foreign assistance and the categories of expenses financed by the
Government Budget, making availability of counterpart funds difficult, even impossible.
Furthermore, continuity of the entire investment program effort for the sector that results from
foreign aid may be at risk. Therefore, to sum up, the main sources of health services financing are: the
Government Budget, (about 13 percent), community participation and social security (about 2 percent),
and international cooperation (about 85 percent).
VI. 1   Contribution by the Government
The Government earmarks about 8 percent of the General State Budget for the health sector; this
covers 13 percent of the total of health expenditures. Recurring expenses are financed via the Ministry
of Public Health and investment expenses via the State Secretariat of Planning.
VI.1.1 Recurring expenses
About 84 percent of the recurring expenses of the Ministry of Public Health fall under three
principal headings: employee compensation (60 percent), travel (13 percent), and hospital meals (11
percent). Drugs represent 5 percent and maintenance of infrastructures and equipment 1 percent. This
distribution by the various expense categories is unbalanced, and has obvious consequences as regards
the need for maintenance and repair of the infrastructure and equipment.



- 52 -
ANNEX 2-4
Page 12 of 36
The problem is less serious as regards drugs since the domestic share of the total annual estimated
needs of US$1.5 million is only US$70,000.
With a view to correcting these distortions, the Ministry of Public Health will redistribute funds
among the various categories of recurring expenses, but that action will not be sufficient by itself. It will
also be important to find ways to increase the total package of financial resources allocated to the sector.
Three lines of action are anticipated in this regard: (a) redistribution of the General State Budget to
benefit the health sector to a greater extent; (b) immediate implementation of a system for recovering the
costs of hospital services and drugs; (c) channeling of part of the foreign assistance into expenditure
categories that are somewhat neglected in the Ministry of Public Health's budget. We must particularly
face up to the infrastructure and equipment rehabilitation plans, including operation, maintenance, and
repair expenses.
VI. 1.2 Investment expenses
These depend almost entirely on foreign assistance. The Government provided only 4 percent
of this kind of outlay in 1989; this contribution was made via a counterpart budget, nourished irregularly
by the National Development Fund managed by what was then the Ministry of Planning. In order to
overcome this situation, a political decision was made to make recovery of costs an essential component
of the Financial Plan of the Ministry of Public Health, since it constituted the most credible solution for
financing health services.
From this standpoint: (a) the Bamako Initiative, successfully launched on an experimental basis
in two regions of the country, will be implemented nationwide; (b) recovery of the costs of hospital
services will be practiced throughout the country, with the proviso that services are to be rationalized and
optimal use made of qualified personnel to provide those services; (c) voluntary participation by the
community, traditionally known as ABOTA, will be encouraged. Although small community health funds
administered by the communities themselves presently account for less than 1 percent of total financing
for the sector, ABOTA actions are important because they fuel the health system at the community level.
VI.2   Contributions from External Cooperation
T he Ministry of Public Health benefits from an international assistance program whose use is still
rather significantly influenced by the philosophy of the donors, and which impacts particularly on project
financing that covers about 75 percent of the recurring expenses (personnel, nondurables, other kinds of
goods and services) and about 25 percent of the investment expenses. About 60 percent of foreign
assistance is comprised of donations; the rest usually takes the form of loans under favorable and
acceptable conditions.
The leading multilateral cooperation agencies in the health field are WHO, UNICEF, UNDP,
World Bank, EC, UNFPA, UNCDF, and the ADB. Major bilateral sources are Sweden, The Republic
of China, The Netherlands, Denmark, France, Cuba, and Portugal. Principal NGO sources include
CUAMM, DCA, Radda Barnen, ASCOM, CECI, AIFO, GVC, MANITESE, the Calouste Gulbenkian
Foundation, the Catholic Church, the Evangelical Churches, and others.
In this context, the Ministry of Public Health drew up a Financial Plan for the Health Sector,
whose main guidelines are:
*     Implementation of costs recovery;
*     Increasing the Government Budget's contribution to health;
*     Expanding the Bamako initiative throughout the country;
*     Redistributing financial resources among the different categories of recurring
expenses;



-53 -ANNEX  2-4
Page 13 of 36
*      Increasing the percentage of foreign aid allocated to recurring expenses,
particularly to categories of expenses less favorably treated by current budgetary
appropriations;
*      Creation of truly effective means of coordinating, controlling, and optimizing the
execution of foreign aid for the health sector.
Given the present conditions of financial austerity, the financial plan for the health sector is
limited to priority projects and areas of intervention that have a direct impact on raising the quality of
services rendered and expanding the coverage of sanitation services, with a view to bettering the state of
the nation's health and, consequently, improving the current health indicators.
VI.3   Policy of Cost Recovery
Attaining the objectives defined by the national health policy depends on two principal sources
of financing, the Ministry of Public Health budget and international assistance. But in the context of
implementing the primary health care program, whose strategy requires future participation of the
beneficiaries, the Ministry has formulated new guidelines, not only for the policy of developing primary
health care, but also with a view to recovering part of the cost of such services. In this context, there
are two cost recovery systems, a financial contribution from the "Abota" system and the Bamako
Initiative. These can be defined in the following terms:
1)    Financial Contribution from the 'Abota" System
Abota is the fee paid by a group of people from a community in exchange for access to a given
service. This contribution is earmarked for the purchase of drugs to ensure supply at the Basic Health
Units.
The form of the contribution varies from one community to the next. In certain places the elderly
pay, while in other places all of the population five years of age and over participates in financing the
system in order to be able to benefit from the services. Those who do not pay this contribution pay the
full cost of treatment when they use the Basic Health Units.
Unfortunately, these contributions cover only 10% of the costs for drugs incurred by these units.
The strategy seeks not only financial participation, but more importantly encourages the population to
participate in improving its health conditions. Thus in some regions the population contributes more than
is necessary, which shows that people are willing to pay more. In this system patients transferred from
one level to another are exempt from additional payments.
2.     Bamako Initiative
The Bamako Initiative is a strategy to get populations to participate in financing health services.
Its objective is first to create an effective primary health care system, and also to win credibility in the
eyes of the local populations, ensuring them quality health care and getting them to participate in this new
system for financing services.
This project has been in an experimental phase in the Gabu region since 1989. But the shortage
of qualified personnel and degradation of the health facilities and equipment have made it impossible to
expand it to the other regions of the country.
The revenues from this initiative are divided as follows:
-      50% is used to replenish the stock of drugs;
-      25% remains at the regional office to ensure part of current expenditures for supervision;
-      20%  returns to the local level to support possible expenses at the initiative of the
population in the health area served;



- 54 -                       ANNEX 2-4
Page 14 of 36
5% is used as an incentive for the technical personnel.
The 50% used to replenish the stock of drugs is deposited in a bank account to be converted to
foreign exchange so as to ensure the supply of drugs.
This policy made possible the following gains in the Gabd region:
1) Improvement of the quality of care, preventing the stock of drugs from becoming depleted;
2) Improvement of the system for maintenance of the equipment, in particular means of
transportation;
3) Improvement of the quality of continuing training through permanent supervision;
4) Testing the capacity of the populations to participate in the financing of health services;
5) Creation and adequate operation of the regional health management team.
It has been found that the population is willing to pay more if necessary, so long as the variety
and quality of services is increased.
In practice the Government decided to include cost recovery in all health units in the country,
including the regional hospitals and National Hospital.
In effect, the calendar provides for the implementing decrees relating to expansion of the Bamako
Initiative and to cost recovery and hospital services--such as clinical analyses, specialized services in the
regional hospitals, National Hospital, and Center for Tropical Medicine-to be promulgated during the
first quarter of 1993. Implementation should begin soon thereafter (second quarter of 1993). An
evaluation will be undertaken at the end of 1993.
VI.3  BUDGET OF THE MINISTRY OF PUBLIC HEALTH
In 1982, the Ministry of Public Health was allocated a budget in the amount of PG
8,625,700,000.00, including the increments, which represents 11.7% of the national budget
(73,527,000,000.00).
Considering the social sector (health) as priority, for 1993 the National People's Assembly approved a
health sector budget of PG 15,373,410,000.00, which represents 9% of the national budget.
V.5   Nonetheless, as a general policy based on cost recovery, the Ministry of Public Health this year
will take in funds that will be allocated primarily to maintenance of infrastructure and equipment, to
strengthening essential drugs, and to workers' incentives, especially in the remote areas.

REPUBLIC OF GUINEA BISSAU
SOCIAL SECTOR PROJECT
In - Service Training Refresher Courses
1.    IDENTIFICATION
Program No.:                         IST-1
Name:                                Management of Health Services Seminars
Total Number of participants:        9 x 12 = 108
Number of participants per Seminar:  12
Number of Weeks for the Entire Program: 30
Number of hours per week:            35
Number of weeks per Seminar:         I
Teachers/Consultants involved:       Foreign: 1; Local: 3
Location:                            Bissau and all regions (8)
Requirements:                        Participants should be member of the Regional Health Teams as
defined by the district teams in WHO documents.
Prerequisite:                        Participation in all three seminars
2.    DESCRIION
Brief Description. During a period of three (3) years, each region with groups of twelve participants will
attend three (3) seminars on management and planning of health services. These seminars will be prepared with
the assistance of one foreign consultant financed by WHO. The consultant will be staying in Bissau for a period
of six weeks and will return thereafter to monitor (4 weeks) and evaluate (2 weeks) the ongoing program.
Between seminars, the participants will be asked to implement the planned activities in their own work
environment. This will be subject of periodic supervision under the seminar on Management in Health Services
(see IST I-A).
Educational Objectives: At the end of the seminar, the participant will be able to: (a)describe the situation
of the health services in their regions and assess its functional capacity. (b) Elaborate an annual workplan for
the region indication priorities and how to overcome some of the major bottlenecks. (c)lmprove general
management (personnel/material/equipment and money) both at regional level as in the Health Centers and in the
USBs.
Methods: Practical teaching methods will be emphasized, starting by analyzing the daily work situation
through exercises, case-studies, presentation and group discussion). Although the cognitive aspect is important,
practical skills and behavioral changes will be strengthened, so that maximal application is guarantee. The
curriculum will be prepared with the assistance of the foreign consultant provide through WHO.
3.    SPECIFIC NEEDS
Didactic Equipment:  Portable equipment: projectors, screens
Other Equipment:    Computer; photocopiers; typewriters; calculators
Consumables:        Standard (paper, pens, etc.)
4.   PROPOSAL FOR PROGRAM CONTENTS
The final curriculum should be provided to the Bank.

1.   IDENTIFICATION
Program No.:                          IST-1 A
Name:                                 Management in Health Services: Supervision
Total Number of participants:
Number of hours per week:
Number of participants:               9 x 12 =108
Number of Weeks for the Entire Programn:
Number of Site Visits:                48
Number of days per Visit:             3
Number of days for the entire program:   144
Teachers/Consultants involved:        Foreign: 1; Local: 3
Location:                             Bissau and all regions
Requirements:                         Participants should be trainees of the IST Seminars.
Prerequisite:                         Participation in all three seminars
2.    DESCRIPTION
Brief Description. During a period of three years, each region with groups of twelve participants will
attend three seminars on management and planning of health services (see IST 1). These seminars will be
prepared with the assistance of one foreign consultant financed by WHO. The consultant will be staying in Bissau
for a period of six weeks and will return thereafter to monitor (4 weeks) and evaluate (2 weeks) the ongoing
program. Between seminars, the participants will be asked to implement the planned activities in their own work
environment. IST 1 (supervision) is meant to indicate the monitoring and supervisory process of the Management
in Health Services I (see above). For educational objectives and teaching methods see IST I (above).
3.    SPECIFIC NEEDS
See IST-I
4.    PROPOSAL FOR PROGRAM CONTENTS
The final curriculum should be provided to the Bank.

1.   IDENTIFICATION
Program No.:                       IST-2
Name:                              Computer Training
Total Number of Students:          20
Nuniber of hours per week:         35
Number of participants:            20
Number of Seminars:                I
Number of days for the entire program: 25
Teachers/Consultants involved:     Foreign: 1; Local: 2 or 3
Location:                          Bissau
Requirements:                      Minimal 9 years of formal education, responsible at regional or
national level for health information systems and maintenance of data
bases.
Prerequisite:                      Application and use of the information acquired during the course.
2.    DESCRIPTI1ON
Brief Description. A group of 20 participants will attend basic computer skills training for a period of
four (4) weeks (DOS, WordPerfect/WordStar, LOTUS 123, DBASE).
Educational Objectives: At the end of the course, the participant will be able to: (a)Create a simple
database for daily use at regional and central level. (b) Use basic software programs (WP5. 1/LOTUS 123) to
monitor health information systems at regional and national level (utilization of services/epidemiological
data/health manpower planning and basic writing skills).  (c)  Apply these skills in their own working
environment.
Methods: Based on the existing collection systems, simple databases should be created by the participants
that will enable them to monitor the health situation in their areas. Practical use of the computer must be
emphasized. The curriculum furnished is specifically to the health services and should be elaborated by 2
consultants, one with specific experience in computer training, the other in the specific area of health and health
information systems.
3.    SPECIFIC NEEDS
Didactic Equipment: Portable computers and printers (including software) for use at regional and national
levels, technical details to be specified.
Other Equipment:    Photocopiers
Consumables:        Standard (computer paper, printers, etc).
4.    PROPOSAL FOR PROGRAM CONTENTS
The final curriculum should be provided to the Bank.

1.   IDENTIFICATION
Program No.:                          IST-3
Name:                                 Statistics for Health Center Nurses
Total Number of Students:             175
Number of hours per week              35
Number of participants:              25/class
Number of Seminars:                  7
Number of weeks per Seminar:          2
Number of days for the entire program:   90
Teachers/Consultants involved:        Foreign: 0; Local: 2
Location:                             Bissau and all regions
Requirements:                         Participants should be Health Center Nurses in charge of statistics.
Prerequisite:                         None
2.    DESCRIPTION
Brief Description: During a period of two (2) weeks, a group of 25 participants will attend in the region
where they work a seminar on simple statistics and health information systems (HIS).
Education Objectives: At the end of the seminar the HD-nurses should be able to: (a) draw a map of
the area where they work, indicating the villages, distances, roads etc.; (b) Elaborate simple indicators for their
HC (coverage, attendance, waiting time etc); (c)Provide a list of all health statistics and HIS that they provide
to the regional levels, indicating its purpose and use; (d)Indicate how they could apply this information in order
to improve health center performance; (e)Analyze and use HC data for internal monitoring and eventual
corrective action.
Methods: Practical teaching methods from daily reality should be emphasized (exercises, case-studies,
presentation and group discussions).
The curriculum will be prepared by a local consultant together with the Training director.
3.    SPECIFIC NEEDS
Didactic Equipment:  Portable equipment: projectors, screens
Other Equipment:    Calculators
Consumables:       Standard (paper, pens, etc)
4.    PROPOSAL FOR PROGRAM CONTENTS
Special Request. The final curriculum should be provided to the Bank.

1.   IDENTIFICATION
Program No.:                          IST-4
Name:                                 Statistics and Health Information Systems for RegionalNational
Technical Staff
Total Number of participants:         25
Number of hours per week              35
Number of participants:              25
Number of Weeks per Seminar:         2
Number of Seminars:                   1
Number of days for the entire Program:   20 (incI. preparations)
Teachers/Consultants involved:        Foreign: 0; Local: 2
Location:                             Bissau
Requirements:                         Participants should all have responsibility in statistics.
Prerequisite:                         9 years of formal education
2.    DESCRIPON
Brief Description. A group of 25 technicians will review statistics and information systems for health
services.
Educational Objectives. At the end of the course, participants will be able to: (a)analyze data from the
regions and draw operational conclusions; (b)Analyze weaknesses in actual health and management information
systems and basic statistics for the health services; (c)Propose simplification in the existing data collection
systems.
Methods. The existing HIS is starting point for the course. Practical teaching methods must be
emphasized (exercises, case-studies, presentations) in order to develop remedial action and if possible a program
to improve the existing HIS.
The curriculum will be prepared under the supervision of the Training Director, eventually by a foreign
consultant assisted by local consultants.
3.    SPECIFIC NEEDS

1.   IDENTIFICATION
Program No.:                         IST-S
Name:                                Training of Trainers Seminar (1TO) for PHC
Total Number of Students:            25
Number of hours per week             35
Number of participants:              25
Number of Seminars:                  1
Number of days per Visit:            3
Number of days for the entire program:   30 (mcl. preparations)
Teachers/Consultants involved:       Foreign:
Location:                            Bissau
Requirements:                        Participants should all have responsibility in CPS at regional levels
and/or in the training of nurses in training ASB's and Matronas
2.    DESCRIPON
Brief Description. A group of 25 CPS responsible/trainers for the supervision and implementation of the
CPS program from all the regions of the country will attend one (1) seminar for a period of three weeks, covering
"training of trainers (TOT) issues'. The seminar will be prepared and provided by 2 foreign consultants who X
will spend a one week in Bissau to prepare the program.
Educational Objectives: At the end of the course participants wiJl be able to: (a)Develop training
objectives for TOT courses to HC nurses, based on their observed performance criteria; (b)conduct training
sessions for nurses, that have to train ASB's and Matronas.
Methods: The curriculum that is to be provided to the hc Nurses will be prepared by the consultants
together with the participants. Practical exercises and role plays to improve skills and performances will be
included as well as attention for general pedagogical issues.
3.    SPECIFIC NEEDS
Didactic Equipment: to be defined by TA
Consumables:       Standard (paper, pens, etc)
4.    PROPOSAL FOR PROGRAM CONTENTS
The final curriculum should be provided to the Bank. The foreign consultants must have direct working
experience in Africa in TOT and knowledge of the portuguese language.

1.   IDENTIFICATION
Program No.:                          IST-6
Name:                                 Financing Health Systems
Total Number of participants:         50
Number of hours per week              35
Number of participants:               50
Number of Seminars:                   I
Number of days for the entire program:   6
Teachers/Consultants involved:        Foreign: 2; Local: 0
Location:                             Bissau
Requirements:                         Regional and National Staff
2.    DESCRIPTION
Brief Description. In an effort of the MINSAS to define a health financing policy, that will include issues
of cost-recovery and regional management of funds, a health economist and a public health consultant familiar
with the country will be provided. Their findings and suggestions have to be discussed together with national
and regional directors in order to elaborate a national financing policy. This will be done in a one week seminar
in Bissau.
Educational Objectives: The objectives of the seminar will be: (a)To inform participants about the health
financing situation of the country; (b)To share information and experiences elsewhere in Africa; (c)To propose
a strategy and a timetable to implement a national health financing policy for the whole country, including the
capital.
Methods: Participative discussions in working groups together with plenaries will enable the seminar to
reach the objectives mentioned. New experiences and information will be shared.
3.    SPECIFIC NEEDS
Didactic Equipment:  OHP/Flannelgraphs and other material to conduct sessions
Consumables:        Standard (computer paper, printers, etc).
4.    PROPOSAL FOR PROGRAM CONTENTS
Special Request. The final curriculum should be provided to the Bank.
The foreign consultants must have direct working experience in financing problems in the African context
and knowledge of the Portuguese language.

store.

6.DESENVOLVIMENTO DOS XATZRIASI DE ENSINO PARA A SAf)DE
i MEPS )
A prestac&o eficaz dos cuidados de sadde depende da formacao
do pessoal sanitario de todas as categorias: m6dicos, enfermeiros,
parteiras, agentes de sadde do base, auxiliares de laborat6rio e

Esta formagco para ser oficaz deve ser apoiada nos materiais

o audio-visuais). Estes materiais denominados, genericamente, por
M2PS (materiais de ensino para a sadde) devem ser desenhados
especialmente  para  responderem  as  reais  necessidades  dos
professores, estudantes eopessoal sanitario em servigo assim como
As comunidades gue-servem.
Um estudo do Banco Mundial intitulado "Publishing for Schools:
Textbooks and the Less Developed Countries publicado em 1980 (Wa-
shnigton DC, Staff Working Paper nQ 398) conclui t '0 livro do texto
(definido aqui como todos as =ateriais impressos utilizados na
*ducacao) A o Cinico instrumento mais potent, * mais oficaz para
olevar a manter padr8os acad6ticos". Levando isto em consideragio,
o problema da falta de meteriais de treinamento do pessoal da saude
nao pode ser solucionado simplesmente atrav6s de envio, atrav6s dos
correios, dos textos, manuais e ouro. livros de referindia.
Isto porque as necessidades s&ort&o grandes, em- qifantidiade e
variedade do conteudo, linguagem e nivel que os tornam insufcientes
para cobrir as nossas necessidades.
Alem disso, os materiais de formaglo nao podem nunca ser
apropriados ou inteiramente relevantes is necessidades e costumes
locais se sao produzidos, fora deste contexto, num pais

Page 21 of 22
desenvolvido.   Estes materiais sao melhores quando concebidos e
desenvolvidos no pals porque exprimem a lingua e cultura locais e
as necessidades sanitirias.
A unica resposta a este desafio e assistir o Ministerio da
Saude a produzir os materiais que precisa para o seu pessoal
concebendo-os especificamentes para a sua utilizagao nas condig,es
locais.
Os materiais devem ser adaptados nao somente por causa do
problema das diferengas linguisticas ou culturais,  mas tambem
porque o nivel de educagco do pessoal e suas tarefas diferem
grandemente de pais para pals.
Para tornar isto eficaz um esforgo nacionat genuino deve ser
promovido com o empenhamento dos professbres e estudantes da
Faculdade de Medicina, dos Monitores da Escola Tecnica dos Quadros
da Saude, dos supervisores do pessoal e do outro pessoal
experiente.   Isto 4 extremamente indispensavel por envolver os
principais utilizadores dos MEPS que se comprometerao a utiliza-los
de uma forma mais eficaz.
A neste espirito que se criou o CECOMES como base para a
edificagao de uma instituigco nacional para a produ,ao local dos
MEPS a partir da qual as competencias locais e outras facilidades
poderiam ser desenvolvidas e mantidas a longo prazo.
Apesar de ser indispensavel uma assistencia financeira externa
na fase inicial do establecimento do projecto MEPS a fim principal
desta assistencia deveria ser o de promover a autosuficiencia da
instituigco nacional.
E esta auto-suficigncia 6 possivel somente na base de
desenvolvimento, -a longo prazo, da capacidade dos nacionais em
continuamente  produzir e fornecer o8 MEPS tao  necessirios  a
formac,o e educagao continua e para responder as inevitaveis
mudangas nas prioridades do sector da saude.   Isto segnifica
maximizar as competencias para conceber, produzir e avaliar os
materiais adequados com recursos locais.
Uma assistencia de curta duragao nao 6 vihvel dado que as
referidas competencias e recursos locais nao aguentarao ap6s a
retirada desta assistencia.  Por isso, a enfase deve ser posta,
desde o inicio, na promogco, a longo prazo, da auto-suflciencia e
do reforgo institucional.
0 investimento na capacitagao do pessoal local e um elemento
vital. Competencias muito especiais sao necessirias nomeadamente
na redaccao e edigao de materiais de formagao e de comunicagao, no
desenho e na ilustragao dos textos educacionais e auxiliares audio-
visuais.

Devem  ser  realizados  localmente  cursos  de  formag&o  qua

necessidades e condigces do pals.
E esta solugia podera ser viavel apenas com a implementagia do
projecto "materiais de ensino para a saude " (MEPS).

REPUBLIC OF GUINEA-BISSAU
SOCIAL SECTOR PROJECT
Description of Past Experiences in Community Participation
1.    Following the proposal agreed in the last Aide-Memoire (Feb. 6, 1992, Par. ii.(D). The
Govermment carried out a research in the regions of Bafata and Oio. Consulting community social
and religious leaders.                                              a
2.    In Bafata, communities are generally receptive to cooperate with the construction of nurses
houses. But in Oio, they said that at present, a gratuity cooperation is impossible. Which reinforce
the project policy of providing some basic incentives (food, bicycles, radios, etc...) to compensate
their efforts. It was stressed the importance of adapting the implementation schedule of this
component to the raining season and cycles of demands for workers in rural areas.
REPORT:
A MINPH/B.M. Mission went to Bafata, with the objective of touching the local ancients,
traditional chiefs and committee to involve and participate in the construction of houses for the Health
Technical Staff at CS and Hospital levels. The Mission, according to instruction received from
MINPH contcted the following places:
Bambadinga. Sector's headquarter. The contacted people were:
* Amara Camara - Sector's President
* El-Madji Abdu Sanha - 'Regulo'
* Queba Camara - Counsel
 Malam Nharia - Committee's Member
* Malam Fati -
Situation of the houses for the Health Staff: The sector headquarters holds infrastructure for the
houses of the Health Technical Staff in the ex houses of 'sono' (there are 4 houses, and each one has
2 bedrooms). The roofs are in good condition, however, a general renovation is necessary (doors,
windows, ceiling, bathrooms, kitchens, electrical installation and sealing).
The population and specially the traditional Chiefs are willing to build one more house near
the CS to lessen the journey of the technical staff specially at night a during the rainy season, for the
houses mentioned above are more than 500 meters away from the CS.
Solicitation/Request: 200 liters empty metal barrel for water during the construction.

ANNEX 3-9
Page 2 of 3
Health Center of Fa6onquito and Cambaiu: The contacted people were:
* Carlito - Sector's President
* Sega Balde - Traditional "Regulo'
* Tcherno Coroa Balde - Mussulman Priest
* Bambo Silla - Division Chief
* Midja Balde - Chief of Fojonquito's 'Babanca"
* Sandji Balde
After the explanation of the Mission's objective the contacted people expressed their wish to
materialize the initiative to build houses for the Health Staff to alleviate the difficulties the staftfs
residential problems. It was unanimously agreed that the population would participate in the
construction of those houses.
Solicitation/Reguest: Shovel, Hoe, empty metal barrel for water, rope and bucket.
Contuboe: Sector's headquarter. Contacted people were:
* El-Hadji Braima Sisse - Contuboel Mussulman Priest
* Djenabu Balde - Responsible of UDEMU
* Sassau Sisse - Brother of the Mussulman Priest
The meeting took place at the principal Mohammedan temple of Contuboel and after brief
explanation the Mussulman Priest made public the idea of start the job as soon as he is allowed. On
the other hand, at the Mission's point o view, there is an old house in contuboel that belongs to
MINPH, where the head of the nurses is residing. This house needs some general repairs. It has the
same problems with distance as the ones encountered in Banbadinca.
Cos: Contacted people:
* Bacar Balde - Traditional "Regulo"
* El-Hadji Sori Djale - Sector's President
After the explanation of the Mission's objective, the traditional 'Regulo' said that the
population will build one more house for the health staff to satisfy the number of staff that the
hospital has.
The situation of the houses in Cosse are identical to the situation of the houses in Bambadica
(there are four old houses that need general repairs).
Sare Baca: These Health Centers have houses for its staff (4 Health expert), therefore, there is no
need to build more houses.
Conclusions: Send a technician to survey the repairs needed for the Health Centers in Bambadinca,
Contuboel and Cosse. The contacted population are aware of the philosophy presented.
Mission Delegate
El-Hadji laia Embalo-Enfo.

Contact Mission with Politic/Administrative authorities of the Oio Remion
Objective: Inquire about the possibility of community participation on the construction of buildings (shelter) for
the health staff.
On may 7, 1992, according to instructions rwceived from MINPH's magement, I went to Oio region
to contact (for the 10th. time) the Regional Chief, Mr. Domingos da Silva, and his adviser, cooperative doctor
of the ONG D.C.A.
After the exposure of the subject and tho idea of MINPH's mangement about the probable location
for the construction, it was discussed tho real noocesities to conclude that:
* 3 buildings of 2 dwelling in Bissor;
* 1 building of 2 dwelling in Binar;
* 2 buildings of 2 dwelling in Mansoa;
* 2 buildings of 2 dwelling in Farim;
* 1 building of 2 dwelling in Guidadge;
* 1 building of 2 dwelling in Mansaba.
We visited the president of the state committee of Bissora region and in the presence of his staff we
stated the problem and received the following reply:
* For Binar region it will b easy to mobilize the population for voluntar work of this nature; for Bissor, it
will be a ittle difficult without counting with the people from the tabncas  outside the city. It will be
necessary to gurantee trnsportation, food, some drinks and tobacco for these people that would be coming
from the tabancas.
* Conceming the land, the president of the Chamber could answer for the necessities. The president Bacar
Djalan talked about the need to set up a starting date for the civil work because the time for the land cultivation
is arriving, therefore, it will not be possible to find avaiable population.
* We contacted Mrs. Stu Djassi, president of the Committee, in Mansa and explained the issue. She
appeared favorable, however, apprhensive about the strting time of the civil work.
* In Mansoa we contacted Mr. Wagnatchuda, President of the Sector's Committee, who believed it possible the
participation of the people, however, it would be necesry to know the starting date of the job in order to
mobilize the people that, at the moment, it in't usy to do.
* In Farim, we contacted Mr. Jaime Sampa, President of the Sector's Committee of Oia region, who after the
knowledge of the subject, said that voluntary and costless population participation for government services, at
this moment, would not be possible. He also explained that voluntary and costless pnctices has long been
banished in Farim, and for them (Farim's people), nobody does anything for the government for free, even with
the knowledge that it is for your own benefit.
* In the region of Biombo, it was not possible to contact anyone so far for the Region's principal and Health
principal were not present this week.
In general, both people appeared hesitant about a voluntary work with the population in the situation
that the country is at the moment.

SOCIAL SECTOR PROJECT
Detailed Proposal from Government on Basic Health Units
1. Introduction.
Le projet "santd de Base" a comnmeenc en 197'7 comme projet
pilote de sante communautaire dans 2 regions du pays, Tombali
et Cacheu. Aujourdhui, on compte un total de 544 Unites de
Sante de Base, distribuees sur toute l'etendue du territoire
de la Guine-Bissau.
A la suite d'un processus de sensibilisation des villages
sur leurs probl6mes de sante, des cases de sante ( Unidades de
Sadde de Base ) ont ete construites par les villageois eux,
m6mes, des comites de sante ont etd formes et des villageois
ont ete sdlectionnes afin d'etre formes commO Agent de Sante
de Base ou Matrone. En grande partie, ce projet reposait sur.
l'initiative des populations locales: le Ministbre de la Santd
Publique fournissait quelques materiaux de construction (
fermetures, portes, equipements et parfois aussi du zinc pour
la couverture de la case) et a mis & la disposition des
r6gions des infirmiers-superviseurs/animateurs pour la suivi
du projet.
Les agents villageois ne sont pas remundrds, meme pas
pendant leur piriode de formation ou recyclage, oti co sont les
villages qui les prennent en charge. II est & noter qu'en
moyenne, il y a environ 3 agents de sante et 3 matrones formes
pour chaque unite de sante de base, donc par village
(tabanca), ainsi evitant que leur charge soit trop lourde.
Au plan curatif, les agents de santd de base sont formes
pour traiter les affections plus communes: le paludisme, la
c9njunctivite, la toux, les diarrhees, les plaies et des
douleurs. Pour d'autres maladies, qui depassent leur niveau de
competence, ils jouent un role intermediaire important dans
l'Avacuation aux echelons superieurs. Bien que l'attraction
pour le curatif et la dispensation de medicaments soit un
fact-ur tr&s important dans la motivation des agents de santA
de base, & partir d'ici ils gagnent aussi la confiance des
villageois pour mener & bien d'autres actions dans le domaine
-preventif: ddpistage des personnes avec un toux persistante,
conseils pour prevenir les piqures de moustiques,
rassemblement des enfants et femmes cibles pour le PEV lors
des visites de l'infirmier au village, education & l'hygiene
et des bonnes pratiques nutritionnelles, etc.
Les matrones, de leur c6te, sont formees pour accompagner
les accouchements dans des conditions hygieniqtes. A travers
des pictogrammes, developpees & cet effet, elles suivent. les
femmes enceintes dans la periode prenatale, dispensant
systematiquement du fer et de la chloroquine comme mesure



- 137 -
ANNEX 3-11
prdventive antiandmique et antipaludique, at identifiant        Page 2 of 10
quelques indicateurs de haute risque obstetrique.
L'exp$rience du "Abota", un systene & paiement pr6alable
qui fonctionne comme une assurance collective, a fait l'objet
de plusieurs etudes dans la literature de santd publique
tropicale.- Ces fonds sont totalement autogeres (comit6 de
sante). Les medicaments sont vendus aux USB & des prix
subventionnes, ce qui fait que le niveau net de recouvrement
des codts est modeste, encore accentue par les d6valuations du
peso guineen des dernibres annees. Entretemps, il est claire
que les villageois sont prets & payer beaucoup plus, pourvu
qu'un approvisionnement regulier en m6dicaments soit garanti.
Sur le total des coits recurrents, difficile & estimer, la
partie des medicaments ne semble neammoins pas preponderante.
2. Description et -ustification du groiet.
Le projet proposd a trois objectifs:
(a) Rendre les Unites de Sante de Base plus
solide et plus durable quant a leur
construction ohysicue.
Les USB sont normalement erig4es avec du materiel local:
murs de terre, et couverture de paille. Ni pour le pavement,
ni pour crepir les murs, on n'utilise du ciment, di aux frais
supplementairs que cela engendre. Les consequences pour la
maintenance et la proprete des locaux est evident, surtout en
saison pluvieuse.
On a estimA qu'avec 25 sacs de ciment par case da sant4,
on serait capable de crepir leas murs et de construire un
pavement cinente au dessus du sol, ainsi rendant la
construction plus durable et facilitant l'hygiene des locaux.
La paille, utilisee comme couverture, doit etre
renouvel6e chaque annee; quand ce ne sont pas les termites,
clest le feu qui est a la base d'un fin precoce de la case. IA
od les moyens le permettent, le zinc est utilise comme
couverture, mais , autant prefere, ce matiriel est aussi plus
cher. En plus, dans certains parties du pays la paille n'est
pas facile a trouver. Le nombre de feuilles necessaire pour
couvrir une USB a ete estimn a 100.
Dans le tableau 1, le nombre de USB necessitant d'un toit
en tole de zinc a iti indiqud pour chaque region; il en est de
mime pour les USB necessitant d'un pavement cimenti. La
tableau 2 donne une recapitulations de cas donnees:
